She heads back to the kitchen, and Ted follows her with his eyes.Then he finds me on his couch, and he tries to sneer through his grin: \"WHEN ARE YOU LEAVING? HUH?\"...JESUS. YOU'RE LIKE THE GODDAMN RUSSIAN SECRET POLICE!\"...OKAY, BYE! YEAH, SURE, GOODBYE!\"Ted walks me out to the driveway. As I start the car, Lou's face is a smile in the window, and Ted is bent at his belly, grabbing their new dalmatian puppy, tickling it with his big hands while the dog rolls and paws the air. And as I ease the car into gear, I hear Ted's voice behind, cooing, very quiet now: \"Do I love this little dog, huh?....Yes, this little shittin' dog....Yes, yes I love you....Yes, I do.\"","wordCount":14518,"articleSection":"Sports","image":[{"@type":"ImageObject","height":1200,"thumbnail":"https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-in-uniform-news-photo-1661945818.jpg?crop=0.780xw:1.00xh;0.219xw,0&resize=100:*","url":"https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-in-uniform-news-photo-1661945818.jpg?crop=0.780xw:1.00xh;0.219xw,0&resize=1200:*","width":1200},{"@type":"ImageObject","height":900,"thumbnail":"https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-in-uniform-news-photo-1661945818.jpg?crop=0.780xw:1.00xh;0.219xw,0&resize=100:*","url":"https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-in-uniform-news-photo-1661945818.jpg?crop=1xw:0.96232xh;center,top&resize=1200:*","width":1200},{"@type":"ImageObject","height":675,"thumbnail":"https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-in-uniform-news-photo-1661945818.jpg?crop=0.780xw:1.00xh;0.219xw,0&resize=100:*","url":"https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-in-uniform-news-photo-1661945818.jpg?crop=1xw:0.72174xh;center,top&resize=1200:*","width":1200}],"mainEntityOfPage":{"@id":"https://www.esquire.com/sports/a5379/biography-ted-williams-0686/","@type":"WebPage"},"publisher":{"@type":"NewsMediaOrganization","name":"Esquire","sameAs":["https://x.com/esquire","https://www.tiktok.com/@esquire","https://www.youtube.com/c/esquire","https://www.facebook.com/Esquire","http://instagram.com/esquire","http://www.pinterest.com/esquiremag/","https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Esquire_(magazine)"],"logo":{"@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://www.esquire.com/_assets/design-tokens/esquire/static/images/logos/logo-jsonld.f471601.png","width":312,"height":60},"address":{"@type":"PostalAddress","postalCode":"10019","addressRegion":"NY","streetAddress":"300 class="css-zxq36b e3morl61"><a data-theme-key="base-link" itemprop="item" href="/sports/" data-vars-ga-ux-element="Breadcrumbs" data-vars-ga-call-to-action="Sports" itemprop="itemListElement" itemscope itemtype="https://schema.org/ListItem" class="css-zxq36b e3morl61"><a data-theme-key="base-link" itemprop="item" href="/esquire-classic-exclusives/" data-vars-ga-ux-element="Breadcrumbs" data-vars-ga-call-to-action="Esquire Classic Exclusives" data-vars-ga-outbound-link="https://www.esquire.com/esquire-classic-exclusives/" class="e3morl60 itemtype="https://schema.org/ListItem" class="css-huh8md e3morl61"><a data-theme-key="base-link" itemprop="item" href="/sports/a5379/biography-ted-williams-0686/" data-vars-ga-ux-element="Breadcrumbs" data-vars-ga-call-to-action="What Do You Think of Ted Williams Now?" data-vars-ga-outbound-link="https://www.esquire.com/sports/a5379/biography-ted-williams-0686/" class="e3morl60 css-10e6ywj e1c1bym14"><span itemprop="name">What Do You Think of Ted Williams Now?</span></a><meta itemprop="position" content="3"/></li></ol></nav><h1 class="css-vnsb5v e1f1sunr8"><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-before" class="css-0 eagam8p0"></span>What Do You Think of Ted Williams Now?<span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-after" class="css-0 eagam8p1"></span></h1><div class="css-1ts17ck e1f1sunr6"><p>Regarded as perhaps the finest piece of sportswriting on record, Richard Ben Cramer's furious saga of Teddy Ballgame is an unmatchable remembrance for an American icon.</p></div><div class="css-1u27fwi e1f1sunr5"><address class="css-8md42a e1v8cotw2"><span data-theme-key="by-line-name" class="css-15ar40z e1v8cotw1"><span>By <a data-theme-key="base-link" href="/author/8804/richard-ben-cramer/" class="css-103hk4r e1c1bym14">Richard Ben Cramer</a></span></span><time class="css-u9tu4i e1f1sunr4">Published: Jan 8, 2013</time></address></div><div><div class="css-hoi8wi e1fy5ocq1"><div data-journey-blur="true" aria-label="Quick actions" role="toolbar" class="content-header-actions css-jwbofi e1fy5ocq4"><div class="css-mm8m61 e1fy5ocq2"><button data-theme-key="action-toolbar-save-button" aria-label="Save Article" aria-pressed="false" class="contentSaveButton"><img data-dynamic-svg="true" src="/_assets/design-tokens/fre/static/icons/saved.ad81f1a.svg?primary=%2523FF3A30" loading="lazy" data-testid="dynamic-svg-base" height="24" width="24" alt="bookmarks" data-theme-key="save-button-icon" class="css-1exgad7 eah8dyz2"/></button></div><div class="css-1m4oxn6 e1fy5ocq3"></div><div class="css-mm8m61 e1fy5ocq2"><div aria-label="Share article" aria-haspopup="true" class="no-print css-w7h146 ejynsbr7"><button data-theme-key="modal-trigger" type="button" data-state="closed" aria-expanded="false" aria-haspopup="dialog" class="ejynsbr3 e91i7z93 css-q6vft3 e12nq69g1"><img src="/_assets/design-tokens/fre/static/icons/social/sharing.89c15b8.svg" alt="Open share options" width="22" height="24" decoding="async" loading="lazy" class="css-dws89z ejynsbr2"/></button></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></header><div data-journey-hide="true" class="longform-container content-container article-container css-km6odw et2g3wt2"><div data-journey-body="longform-article" class="article-body-content article-body longform-body css-n3hm4j et2g3wt6"><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="0" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60"><em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="0.0">This story was originally published in the June 1986 issue of Esquire. To read every Esquire story ever published, upgrade to <a href="https://shop.esquire.com/esquire-all-access-membership-2.html?cds_tracking_code=esqengredirect" target="_blank" data-vars-ga-outbound-link="https://shop.esquire.com/esquire-all-access-membership-2.html?cds_tracking_code=esqengredirect" data-vars-ga-ux-element="Hyperlink" data-vars-ga-call-to-action="All Access" data-vars-membership-link="https://shop.esquire.com/esquire-all-access-membership-2.html?cds_tracking_code=esqengredirect" data-node-id="0.0.1" data-href="https://shop.esquire.com/esquire-all-access-membership-2.html?cds_tracking_code=esqengredirect" class="css-144f60g e1aq0z090">All Access</a>.</em></p><hr data-node-id="1" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="2" class="body-text css-i9p093 emevuu60"><em data-node-id="2.0">Few men try for best ever, and Ted Williams is one of those. There's a story about him I think of now. This is not about baseball but fishing. He meant to be the best there, too. One day he says to a Boston writer: &quot;Ain't no one in heaven or earth ever knew more about fishing.&quot;</em></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="3" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60"><em data-node-id="3.0">&quot;Sure there is,&quot; says the scribe.</em></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="4" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60"><em data-node-id="4.0">&quot;Oh, yeah? Who?&quot;</em></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="5" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60"><em data-node-id="5.0">&quot;Well, God made the fish.&quot;</em></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="6" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60"><em data-node-id="6.0">&quot;Yeah, awright,&quot; Ted says. &quot;But you have to go pretty far back.&quot;</em></p><hr data-node-id="7" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="8" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="8.0">It was forty-five years ago,</strong> when achievements with a bat first brought him to the nation's notice, that Ted Williams began work on his defense. He wanted fame, and wanted it with a pure, hot eagerness that would have been embarrassing in a smaller man. But he could not stand celebrity. This is a bitch of a line to draw in America's dust.<br data-node-id="8.2"/></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="9" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted was never the kind to quail. In this epic battle, as in the million smaller face-offs that are his history, his instinct called for exertion, for a show of force that would <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="9.1">shut those bastards up</em>. That was always his method as he fought opposing pitchers, and fielders who bunched up on him, eight on one half of the field; as he fought off the few fans who booed him and thousands who thought he ought to love them, too; as he fought through, alas, three marriages; as he fought to a bloody standoff a Boston press that covered, with comment, his every sneeze and snort. He meant to <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="9.3">dominate</em>, and to an amazing extent, he did. But he came to know, better than most men, the value of his time. So over the years, Ted Williams learned to avoid annoyance. Now in his seventh decade, he had girded his penchants for privacy and ease with a bristle of dos and don'ts that defeat casual intrusion. He is a hard man to meet.</p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-18" data-node-id="10" class="embed"><div size="large" data-embed="body-image" class="align-center size-large embed css-uhpotl e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="profile of ted williams playing baseball game" title="Profile of Ted Williams Playing Baseball Game" loading="lazy" width="3807" height="3031" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-boston-red-sox-slugger-is-shown-during-batting-news-photo-1661946436.jpg?resize=640:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-boston-red-sox-slugger-is-shown-during-batting-news-photo-1661946436.jpg?resize=980:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-boston-red-sox-slugger-is-shown-during-batting-news-photo-1661946436.jpg?resize=2048:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-boston-red-sox-slugger-is-shown-during-batting-news-photo-1661946436.jpg?resize=2048:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-1gccgwy e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Bettmann</span><span class="css-1r6mz5z e1geg53v0">//</span><span class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v1">Getty Images</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">Williams batting, 1940s.</div></div></div></div></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="11" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">This is not to paint him as a hermit or a shrinking flower, Garbo with a baseball bat. No, in his hometown of Islamorada, on the Florida Keys, Ted is not hard to <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="11.1">see</em>. He's out every day, out early and out loud. You might spot him at a coffee bar where the guides breakfast, quizzing them on their catches and telling them what <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="11.3">he</em> thinks of fishing here lately, which is &quot;IT'S HORSESHIT.&quot; Or you might notice him in a crowded but quiet tackle shop, poking at a reel that he's seen before, opining that it's not been sold because &quot;THE PRICE IS TOO DAMN HIGH,&quot; after which Ted advises his friend, the proprietor, across the room: &quot;YOU MIGHT AS WELL QUIT USING THAT HAIR DYE. YOU'RE GOING BALD ANYWAY.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="12" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He's always first, 8:00 A.M., at the tennis club. He's been up for hours, he's ready. He fidgets, awaiting appearance by some other, any other, man with a racket, where upon Ted bellows, before the newcomer can say hello: &quot;WELL, YOU WANNA PLAY?&quot; Ted's voice normally emanates with gale and force, even at close range. Apologists attribute this to the ear injury that sent him home from Korea and ended his combat flying career. But Ted can speak softly and hear himself fine, if it's only one friend around. The roar with which he speaks in a public place, or to anyone else, has nothing to do with his hearing. It's your hearing he's worried about.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="13" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted Williams can hush a room just by entering. There is a force that boils up from him and commands attention. This he has come to accept as his destiny and his due, just as he came to accept the maddening, if respectful, way his opponents pitched around him (he always seemed to be leading the league in bases on balls), or the way every fan in the ball park seemed always to watch (and comment upon) T. Williams's every move. It was often said Ted would rather play ball in a lab, where fans couldn't see. But he never blamed fans for watching him. His hate was for those who couldn't or wouldn't <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="13.1">feel</em> with him, his effort, his exultation, pride, rage, or sorrow. If they wouldn't share those, then there was his scorn, and he's make them feel that, by God. These days, there are no crowds, but Ted is watched, and why not? What other match could draw a kibitzer's eye when Ted, on the near court, pounds toward the net, slashing the air with his big racket, laughing in triumphant derision as he scores with his killer drop shot, or smacking the ball twenty feet long and roaring, &quot;SYPHILITIC SON OF A BITCH!&quot; as he hurls his racket to the clay at his feet?</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="14" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">And who could say Ted does not mean it be seen when he stops in front of the kibitzers as he and his opponent change sides? &quot;YOU OKAY?&quot; Ted wheezes as he yells as his foe. &quot;HOW D'YA FEEL?...HOW OLD ARE YOU?...JUST WORRIED ABOUT YOUR HEART HA HA HAW.&quot; Ted turns and winks, mops his face. A kibitzer says mildly: &quot;How are you, Ted?&quot; And Ted drops the towel, swells with Florida air, grins gloriously, and booms back:</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="15" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;WELL, HOW DO I LOOK?...HUH?...WHAT DO YOU THINK OF TED WILLIAMS NOW?&quot; </p><hr data-node-id="16" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="17" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="17.0">It is another matter,</strong> though, to interrupt his tour of life, and force yourself on his attention. This is where the dos and don'ts come in. The dos fall to you. They concern your conduct, habits, schedule, attitude, and grooming. It's too long a list to go into, but suffice it to recall the one thing Ted liked about managing the Washington Senators: &quot;I was in a position where people had to by God <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="17.2">listen</em>.&quot;</p><section data-embed="pullquote" data-lazy-id="P0-19" data-node-id="18" class="embed"><blockquote data-theme-key="pullquote" class="css-1eiql25 e1pe3zr91"><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-before" class="css-5esr6z eagam8p0"></span><blockquote class="css-xw57yn e1pe3zr90">Ted Williams can hush a room just by entering. There is a force that boils up from him and commands attention. </blockquote><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-after" class="css-0 eagam8p1"></span></blockquote></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="19" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">The don'ts, on the other hand, pertain to Ted, and they are probably summed up best by Jimmy Albright, the famous fishing guide, Ted's friend since 1947 and Islamorada neighbor. &quot;Ted don't do,&quot; Jimmy says, &quot;mucha anything he don't want to.&quot;<br data-node-id="19.1"/></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="20" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He does not wait or bend his schedule: &quot;I haven't got my whole career to screw around with you, bush!&quot; He does not screw around with anything for long, unless it's hunting fish, and then he'll spend all day with perfect equanimity. He does not reminisce, except in rare moods of ease. He does not talk about his personal life. &quot;Why the hell should I?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="21" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">His standing in the worlds of baseball and fishing would not net him an invitation a night, but he does not go to dinners. One reason is he does not wear ties, and probably hasn't suffered one five times in a quarter century. Neither does he go to parties, where he'd have to stand around, with a drink in his hand, &quot;listening to a lot of bullshit.&quot; No, he'd rather watch TV.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="22" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He does not go to restaurants, and the reasons are several: They make a fuss, and the owner or cook's on his neck like gnat. Or worse, it's a stream of <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="22.1">sportsfans</em> (still Ted's worst epithet) with napkins to sign. At restaurants you wait, wait, <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="22.3">wait</em>. Restaurants have little chairs and tables, no place for elbows, arms, knees, feet. At restaurants there's never enough food. Lastly, restaurants charge a lot, and Ted doesn't toss money around. (A few years ago he decided $2.38 was top price for a pound of beef. For more than a year, he honed his technique on chuck roast and stew meat. Only an incipient boycott by his friends, frequent dinner guests, finally shook his resolve.)</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="23" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">The last reason is seized upon unkindly by restaurateurs in Islamorada and nearby Keys: &quot;No, he doesn't come in. He's too cheap. He'd go all over town, sonofabitch, and he'd pay by check, hoping they wouldn't cash the check, they'd put it on the wall.&quot;</p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-20" data-node-id="24" class="embed"><div size="large" data-embed="body-image" class="align-center size-large embed css-uhpotl e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="ted williams in airplane cockpit" title="Ted Williams in Airplane Cockpit" loading="lazy" width="2980" height="2822" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/baseball-fans-will-immediately-recognize-this-face-despite-news-photo-1661946385.jpg?resize=640:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/baseball-fans-will-immediately-recognize-this-face-despite-news-photo-1661946385.jpg?resize=980:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/baseball-fans-will-immediately-recognize-this-face-despite-news-photo-1661946385.jpg?resize=2048:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/baseball-fans-will-immediately-recognize-this-face-despite-news-photo-1661946385.jpg?resize=2048:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-1gccgwy e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Bettmann</span><span class="css-1r6mz5z e1geg53v0">//</span><span class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v1">Getty Images</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">Williams in 1952.</div></div></div></div></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="25" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">But this is resentment speaking, and it is Ted's lot in life to be misunderstood. Some are put off, for instance, by the unlisted phone, by the steel fence, the burglar alarm, and KEEP OUT signs that stud his gates when he swings them shut with the carbon-steel chain and the padlock. But his friends think nothing of it. A few have his number, but they don't call, as they know he's got the phone off the hook. No, they'll cruise by; if the gates are unchained, if they see his faded blue truck with the bumper sticker sign IF GUNS ARE OUTLAWED ONLY OUTLAWS WILL HAVE GUNS, if it's not mealtime and not too late and there's nothing they know of that's pissing Ted off, well, then...they drive right in.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="26" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">And this is the way to meet Ted: by introduction of an old friend, like Jimmy Albright. It's Jimmy who knows where to park the car so it won't annoy Ted. It's Jimmy who cautions, as we throw away out cigarettes, that Ted won't allow any smoke in his house. It's Jimmy who starts the ball rolling, calls out &quot;Hiya, Ted!&quot; as the big guy launches himself from his chair and stalks across the living room, muttering in the stentorian growl that passes with him as sotto voce: &quot;Now who the hell is THIS?&quot;<br data-node-id="26.1"/></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="27" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He fills the door. &quot;Awright, come on in. WELL, GET THE HELL IN HERE.&quot; He sticks out a hand, but his nose twitches, lip curls at a lingering scent of smoke. Ted's got my hand, now, but he says to Jimmy: &quot;S'that you who stinks, or this other one, too? Jesus! Awright, sit down. Sit over there.&quot;<br data-node-id="27.1"/></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="28" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted wants to keep this short and sweet. He's in the kitchen, filling tumblers with fresh lemonade. Still, his voice rattles the living room: &quot;D'YOU READ THE BOOK?&quot; He means his memoir, <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="28.1">My Turn at Bat</em>. &quot;Anything you're gonna ask, I guarantee it's in the goddamn book....Yeah, awright. I only got one copy myself.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="29" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Where's the BOOK?&quot; he yells to Louise Kaufman, his mate. Ted thinks that Lou knows the location of everything he wants. &quot;HEY SWEETIES, WHERE'S THAT GODDAMN BOOK?&quot;</p><section data-embed="pullquote" data-lazy-id="P0-21" data-node-id="30" class="embed"><blockquote data-theme-key="pullquote" class="css-1eiql25 e1pe3zr91"><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-before" class="css-5esr6z eagam8p0"></span><blockquote class="css-xw57yn e1pe3zr90">"WELL, HOW DO I LOOK?...HUH?...WHAT DO YOU THINK OF TED WILLIAMS NOW?"</blockquote><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-after" class="css-0 eagam8p1"></span></blockquote></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="31" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Lou has raised three sons, so no man, not even Ted, is going to fluster her. She comes downstairs bearing the book, which she hands to Ted, and which he throws to the floor at my feet. He growls: &quot;Now, I want you to read that. And then I'm gonna ask you a <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="31.1">key question</em>.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="32" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">I ask: &quot;Tomorrow? Should I call?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="33" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;HELL NO.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="34" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Jimmy says he'll arrange a meeting.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="35" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted says: &quot;HOW'S THAT LEMONADE?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="36" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Good.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="37" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;HUH? IS IT?...WELL, WHAT DO YOU THINK OF ME?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="38" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">In the car, minutes later, Jimmy explains that Ted won't talk on the phone. &quot;Ted gimme his number twenty-five years ago,&quot; Jimmy says. &quot;And I never give it yet to any asshole.&quot; We both nod solemnly as this fact settles, and we muse on the subject of trust. I'm thinking of the fine camaraderie between sportsmen and...wait a minute. Jimmy and Ted have been friend forty years now.<br data-node-id="38.1"/></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="39" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Does that make fifteen years Ted <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="39.1">didn't</em> give him the number?</p><blockquote data-node-id="40" class="body-blockquote css-ikpvzp emevuu60">I'm glad it's over. Before anything else, understand that I am glad it's over...I wouldn't go back to being eighteen or nineteen years old knowing what was in store, the sourness and bitterness, knowing how I thought the weight of the damn world was always on my neck, grinding on me. I wouldn't go back to that for anything. I wouldn't want to go back....I wanted to be the greatest hitter who ever lived.... — Ted Williams, with John Underwood: <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="40.1">My Turn at Bat</em></blockquote><hr data-node-id="41" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="42" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="42.0">San Diego was a small town,</strong> and the Williams house was a small box of wood, one story like the rest on Utah Street. It was a workingman's neighborhood, but at the bottom of the Great Depression a lot of men weren't working. Ted's father was a photographer with a little shop downtown. Later he got a U.S. marshal's job, in gratitude for some election favors he'd done for Governor Merriam, and that remained his claim to fame. Ted never saw much of him. His mother was the strength in the family, a small woman with a will of steel who gave her life to the Salvation Army. She was always out on the streets, San Diego or south of the border, the Angel of Tijuana, out fighting the devil drink, selling the <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="42.2">War Cry</em> or playing on a cornet, and God-blessing those who vouchsafed a nickel. Sometimes she'd take along her elder boy, and Ted hated it, but he didn't disobey. He was a scrawny kid and shy, and he tried to shrink behind the bass drum so none of his friends would see. There was school, but he wasn't much good there. History was the only part he liked. And then he'd come home, and his mother was out, and sometimes it was 10:00 at night, and Ted and his brother, Danny, were still on the porch on Utah Street, waiting for someone to let them in.<br data-node-id="42.4"/></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="43" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Soon home lost its place at the center of Ted's life. There wasn't much in the little house that could make him feel special. It wasn't the place where he could be the Ted Williams he wanted to be. North Park playground was a block away, and there, with one friend, a bat, and a ball, Ted could be the biggest man in the majors. The game he played was called Big League: one kid pitched, the other hit to a backstop screen. &quot;Okay, here's the great Charlie Gehringer,&quot; Ted would announce, as he took his stance. Or sometimes it was Bill Terry, Hank Wilson, or another great man he'd never seen. &quot;Last of the ninth, two men on, two out, here's the pitch...<em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="43.1">Gehringer swings</em>!&quot; Ted swung. Crack! Another game-winning shot for the great...<em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="43.3">the Great Ted Williams</em>.</p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-22" data-node-id="44" class="embed"><div size="large" data-embed="body-image" class="align-center size-large embed css-uhpotl e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="ted williams holding fish in boat" title="Ted Williams Holding Fish in Boat" loading="lazy" width="3793" height="3036" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-a-mighty-handy-man-with-a-bat-for-the-boston-news-photo-1661946485.jpg?resize=640:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-a-mighty-handy-man-with-a-bat-for-the-boston-news-photo-1661946485.jpg?resize=980:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-a-mighty-handy-man-with-a-bat-for-the-boston-news-photo-1661946485.jpg?resize=2048:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-a-mighty-handy-man-with-a-bat-for-the-boston-news-photo-1661946485.jpg?resize=2048:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-1gccgwy e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Bettmann</span><span class="css-1r6mz5z e1geg53v0">//</span><span class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v1">Getty Images</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">Williams in the Florida Keys, 1950.</div></div></div></div></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="45" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">They were just the dreams of a kid, that's all. But Ted went back to the playground every day. First it was with a friend his own age, then the playground director, Rod Luscomb, a grown man, a two-hundred-pounder who'd made it to the Cal State League. Ted pitched to Luscomb, Luscomb to Ted. At first they'd always tell each other when they were going to throw a curve. But then Ted started calling out: &quot;Don't tell me, just see if I can hit it.&quot; Crack! Ted could hit it. &quot;Listen, Lusk,&quot; Ted used to say. &quot;Someday I'm going to build myself a ball park with cardboard fences. Then, I'm going to knock 'em all down, every darn one, with home runs.&quot; But Ted wasn't hitting homers with his scrawny chest, those skinny arms. Luscomb set him to push-ups, twenty, then forty, fifty, then a hundred, then fingertip push-ups. Ted did them at home on Utah Street. He picked his high school, Herbert Hoover High, because it was new and he's have a better chance to make the team. When he made it, he came to school with glove hung like a badge on his belt. He carried a bat to class. And after his last class (or before), it was back to the playground. Then in darkness, home for dinner, the push-ups, and the dreams.  </p><hr data-node-id="46" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="47" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="47.0">There were no major leagues</strong> in San Diego. There was no TV. He had no more idea of the life he sought than we have of life on the moon. Maybe less, for we've seen the replays. Ted had to dream it all himself. And how could he measure what he'd give up? He wasn't interested in school, didn't care about cars, or money, or girls. He felt so awkward, except on the field. There, he'd show what Ted Williams could do. Now Hoover High went to Pomona for a doubleheader, and Ted pitched the first game, played outfield in the second, and hit and hit, and Hoover won, and wasn't it great? There was an ice cream cart, and Ted ate eighteen Popsicles. His teammates started counting when he got to ten. But Ted didn't mind them making fun. That's how good he felt: him hitting, and Hoover winning, and the big crowd. Gee, that's the governor! And Ted found himself in the governor's path, the man who'd tossed his father a job, and he had to say something, and the awkwardness came flooding back, he felt red in his face. So Ted grabbed tighter on his bat and he barked at Merriam: &quot;HIYA, GOV!&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="48" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Of course people called him cocky. But he only wondered: Was he good enough? At seventeen, as high school closed, he signed with the local team, the Coast League Padres. They offered him $150 a month and said they'd pay for the whole month of June, even though this was already June 20. So that was Ted's bonus -- twenty days' pay. He didn't care: he was a step closer, and each day was a new wonder.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="49" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He rode the trains, farther from home than he'd ever been. He stayed in hotels with big mirrors, and Ted would stand at a mirror with a bat, or a rolled-up paper, anything -- just to see his swing, how he looked: he had to look good. He got balls from the club, so many that his manager, Frank Shellenback, thought Ted must be selling them. No, Ted took them to his playground, got Lusk and maybe a kid to shag flies, and hit the covers off those balls. </p><section data-embed="pullquote" data-lazy-id="P0-23" data-node-id="50" class="embed"><blockquote data-theme-key="pullquote" class="css-1eiql25 e1pe3zr91"><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-before" class="css-5esr6z eagam8p0"></span><blockquote class="css-xw57yn e1pe3zr90">Ted had to dream it all himself. And how could he measure what he'd give up? </blockquote><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-after" class="css-0 eagam8p1"></span></blockquote></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="51" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Best of all, there were major leaguers, real ones, to see. They were old by the time they came to the Coast League, but Ted watched them, almost ate them with his eyes, measured himself against their size. Lefty O'Doul was managing the San Francisco Seals, and he was one of the greats. Ted stopped Lefty on the field one day. He had to know: &quot;Mr. O'Doul, please...what should I do to be a good hitter?&quot; And Lefty said: &quot;Kid, best advice I can give you is don't let anybody change you.&quot; Ted walked around on air. After that, in bad times, he'd hear O'Doul's voice telling him he'd be okay. The bad times were slumps. If Ted couldn't hit, the world went gray. In his second year with San Diego, Ted hit a stretch of oh-for-eighteen. He hung around the hotel in San Francisco, moping. He didn't know what to do with himself. He got a paper and turned to the sports. There was an interview with O'Doul. The headline said: WILLIAMS GREATEST HITTER SINCE WANER. And Ted thought: I wonder who this Williams is?</p><hr data-node-id="52" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="53" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="53.0">It was a newspaper</strong> that told him, too, about Boston buying his contract. The Red Sox! Ted's heart sank. It was a fifth-place club and as far away as any team could be: cold, northerly, foreign. Still, it was big league, wasn't it?</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="54" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He had to borrow $200 for the trip east; there were floods that spring, 1938. He got to Sarasota, Florida, about a week late. And when he walked into the clubhouse, all the players were on the field.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="55" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Well, so you're the kid.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="56" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">It was Johnny Orlando, clubhouse boy. The way Johnny told it, he'd been waiting for this Williams. &quot;Then, one morning, this Li'l Abner walks into the clubhouse. He's got a red sweater on, his shirt open at the neck, a raggedy duffle bag. His hair's on end like he's attached to an electric switch....'Where you been, Kid?' I asked him. 'Don't you know we been working out almost a whole week? Who you supposed to be, Ronald Colman or somebody, you can't get here on time?'&quot; Johnny gave Ted a uniform, the biggest he had in stock. But as Ted grabbed a couple of bats, his arms and legs stuck out, the shirttail wouldn't stay in his pants.</p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-24" data-node-id="57" class="embed"><div size="large" data-embed="body-image" class="align-right size-large embed css-f5ml56 e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="ted williams" title="Ted Williams (1918-2002), American Baseball Player for Boston Red Sox, full-length Portrait in Baseball Uniform, Harry Warnecke, 1940" loading="lazy" width="802" height="1024" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-american-baseball-player-for-boston-red-sox-news-photo-1662042812.jpg?resize=320:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-american-baseball-player-for-boston-red-sox-news-photo-1662042812.jpg?resize=480:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-american-baseball-player-for-boston-red-sox-news-photo-1662042812.jpg?resize=480:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-american-baseball-player-for-boston-red-sox-news-photo-1662042812.jpg?resize=480:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-78jldq e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Universal History Archive</span><span class="css-1r6mz5z e1geg53v0">//</span><span class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v1">Getty Images</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">Ted Williams, 1940. </div></div></div></div></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="58" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Well, come on, Kid,&quot; Johnny said, and he led the bean pole out to the field. From the first-base stands, a voice yelled: &quot;Hey, busher, tuck your shirt in! You're in the big leagues now.&quot;<br data-node-id="58.1"/></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="59" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted wheeled around, face red. &quot;Who's the wise guy in the stands?&quot; Johnny told him: &quot;That's Joe Cronin, Kid, your manager.&quot; Ted put his head down and made for the outfield. It wasn't the reception he'd expected, but at least he had his nickname. Everyone heard Johnny show him around. &quot;Look here, Kid. Go over there, Kid.&quot; It stuck right away; it was a role, he knew. And soon Joe Cronin would fill the spot Rod Luscomb had held in Ted's life. Cronin was only thirty-one, but that was old enough. He was a hitter and a teacher, a manager, counselor, and Ted was ever the Kid. </p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="60" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Cronin had come from Washington, one of the Red Sox's imported stars. The owner, Tom Yawkey, was buying a contender. Along with Cronin, the Hall of Fame shortstop, Yawkey raided Washington for Ben Chapman, a speedy right fielder and .300 hitter. From the Browns, Yawkey got Joe Vosmik, a left fielder who would hit .324. From the A's, Yawkey bought two old greats, Lefty Grove and Jimmy Foxx, along with Doc Cramer, another .300 hitter, for center field.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="61" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">These were the finest hitters Ted had seen. He couldn't take his eyes off the batter's box. But the presence of all those hitters in camp meant one thing of terrible import to Ted: no nineteen-year-old outfielder was breaking in, not that year, and the veterans let Ted know it. Vosmik, Chapman, and Cramer, rough old boys all of them, made sure he had his share of insults. He lasted about a week, until the club broke camp for the first game in Tampa.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="62" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted wasn't going to Tampa. He was headed to Daytona Beach, where the Minneapolis farm team trained. Ted saw the list and the shame welled up, turned to rage. He yelled to the veteran outfielders: <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="62.1">&quot;I'll be back. And I'll make more money in this fucking game than all three of you combined.&quot;</em> When he walked to the bus stop with Johnny Orlando, he asked: &quot;How much you think those guys make?&quot; And Johnny said: &quot;I don't know, maybe fifteen thousand apiece.&quot; Ted nodded, his mouth set in a grim line. He had his salary goal now. Then he borrowed $2.50 from Johnny for the bus trip to the minors.</p><hr data-node-id="63" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="64" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="64.0">In Minneapolis,</strong> Ted led the league in everything: average, home runs, runs batted in, screwball stunts....There were tales of his conduct in the outfield, where he'd sit down between batters, or practice swinging an imaginary bat, watching his leg-stride, watching his wrist-break, watching everything except balls hit to him. If he did notice a fly ball, he'd gallop after it, slapping his ass and yelling, &quot;HI HO SILVER!&quot; He was nineteen, and fans loved him. But if there was one boo, the Kid would hear it, and he'd try to shut the sonofabitch up for good. Once, when a heckler got on him, Ted fired a ball into the stands -- and hit the wrong guy. That was more than the manager, poor old Donie Bush, could stand. He went to the owner, Mike Kelley, and announced: &quot;That's it. One of us goes. Him or me.&quot; Kelley replied, quick and firm: &quot;Well, then, Donie, it'll have to be you.&quot;</p><section data-embed="pullquote" data-lazy-id="P0-25" data-node-id="65" class="embed"><blockquote data-theme-key="pullquote" class="css-1eiql25 e1pe3zr91"><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-before" class="css-5esr6z eagam8p0"></span><blockquote class="css-xw57yn e1pe3zr90">Someday I'm going to build myself a ball park with cardboard fences. Then, I'm going to knock 'em all down, every darn one, with home runs.</blockquote><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-after" class="css-0 eagam8p1"></span></blockquote></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="66" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">By the time Ted came back to Sarasota, the Red Sox were banking on him, too. They traded Ben Chapman, the right fielder who'd hit .340 the year before. Ted told himself: &quot;I guess that shows what they think of ME.&quot; It was like he had to convince himself he was really big league now. Even after a good day, three-for-four, he'd sit alone in the hotel with the canker of one failure eating at him. If he screwed up, or looked bad, the awkwardness turned to shame, the shame to rage. As the team headed north, Ted was hitting a ton, but it wasn't enough. At the first stop, Atlanta, Johnny Orlando pointed out the strange right-field wall -- three parallel fences, one behind the other. Johnny said: &quot;I saw Babe Ruth hit one over that last fence....&quot; Ted vowed right there he'd do it, too. But the next day, he couldn't clear one fence. Worse still, he made an error. In the seventh, he put the Sox up with a three-run triple, but it wasn't enough. He had to show what Ted Williams could do! When he struck out in the eighth, he went to right field seething. Then a popup twisted toward his foul line. He ran and ran, dropped the ball, then booted it trying to pick it up. Rage was pounding in him. He grabbed the ball and fired it over those right-field walls. By the time the ball hit Ponce de Leon Avenue and bounced up at a Sears store, Cronin had yanked Ted out of the game.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="67" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Even Ted couldn't understand what that rage was to him, why he fed it, wouldn't let it go. He only knew that the next day in Atlanta, he smashed a ball over those three walls and trotted to the bench with a hard stare that asked Johnny Orlando, and anyone else who cared to look: Well, what do you think of the Kid now?</p><hr data-node-id="68" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="69" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="69.0">He had a great first year</strong> in the bigs. On his first Sunday at Fenway Park, he was four-for-five with his first home run, a shot to the bleachers in right-center, where only five balls had landed the whole year before. There were nine Boston dailies that vied in hyperbole on the new hero. TED WILLIAMS REVIVES FEATS OF BABE RUTH, said the <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="69.2">Globe</em> after Ted's fourth game.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="70" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">From every town he wrote a letter to Rod Luscomb with a layout of the ball park and a proud X where his homer hit. He was always first to the stadium and last to leave after a game. He took his bats to the post office to make sure they were the proper weight. He quizzed the veterans mercilessly about the pitchers coming up. &quot;What does Newsom throw in a jam? How about Ruffing's curve?&quot; It was as if he meant to ingest the game. He only thought baseball. On trains, he'd never join the older guys in poker games or drinking bouts. At hotels, it was always room service, and Ted in his shorts, with a bat, at a mirror.</p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-26" data-node-id="71" class="embed"><div size="medium" data-embed="body-image" class="align-center size-medium embed css-y6m8k e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="ted williams" title="ted williams" loading="lazy" width="1786" height="1188" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/screen-shot-2022-09-01-at-10-34-46-am-1662042905.png?resize=640:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/screen-shot-2022-09-01-at-10-34-46-am-1662042905.png?resize=768:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/screen-shot-2022-09-01-at-10-34-46-am-1662042905.png?resize=980:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/screen-shot-2022-09-01-at-10-34-46-am-1662042905.png?resize=980:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-swqnqv e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Esquire</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">The opening spread of this story in the June 1986 issue of Esquire.</div></div></div></div></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="72" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">His roomie was Broadway Charlie Wagner, a pitcher with a taste for fancy suits and an occasional night on the town. One night, 4:00 A.M., Wagner was sleeping the sleep of the just when, <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="72.1">wham</em>, CRASH, he's on the floor, with the bed around his ears, and he figures it's the end. He opens his eyes to see the bean-pole legs, then the shorts, and then the bat. Ted's been practicing and he hit the bedpost. Does he say he's sorry? No, he doesn't say a damn thing to Wagner. He's got a little dream-child smile on his face and he murmurs to himself: &quot;Boy, what power!&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="73" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He ended up hitting .327 and leading the league for runs batted in, the first time a rookie ever won that crown. He finished with thirty-one home runs, at least one in each American League park. There was no rookie of the year award, but Babe Ruth himself put the title on Ted, and that seemed good enough.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="74" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">And after the season, he didn't go home. San Diego had lost its hold. His parents were getting a divorce, and that was pain he didn't want to face. He didn't want to see his troubled brother. He didn't want to see the crummy little house with the stained carpet and the chair with the hole where the mice ate through. He had a car now, a green Buick worth a thousand bucks. He went to Minnesota. There was a girl there he might want to see. Her dad was a hunting guide, and he could talk to her. And there was duck to hunt. As many as he wanted. And he could go where he wanted. And do what he wanted. He was twenty-one. And Big League. </p><hr data-node-id="75" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="76" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="76.0">Everybody knew</strong> 1940 would be a great year. Ted knew he’d be better: now he’d seen the pitchers, he knew he could do it.  Tom Yawkey sent him a contract for $10,000, double his rookie pay. &quot;I guess that shows what they think of ME.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="77" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">No one thought about this, but pitchers had seen Ted, too. And this time around, no one was going to try to blow a fastball by him. Cronin was having an off year and Double-X Foxx was getting old and would never again be batting champ. So the pressure fell to Ted. If they pitched around him and he got a walk, that wasn't enough, the Sox needed hits. If he got a hit, it should have been a homer. A coven of bleacherites started riding Ted. And why not? They could always get a rise. Sometimes he'd yell back. Or he'd tell the writers: &quot;I'm gonna take raw hamburger out to feed those wolves.&quot; The papers rode the story hard: O Unhappy Star! Then he told the writers: &quot;Aw, Boston's a shitty town. Fans are lousy.&quot; Now the papers added commentary, pious truths about the Boston fans as the source of Ted's fine income. So Ted let them have it again: &quot;My salary is peanuts. I'd rather be traded to New York.&quot; That did it. Now it wasn't just a left-field crowd riding Ted. It was civic sport. <em data-node-id="77.1">He doesn't like Boston, huh? Who does he think he is?</em></p><section data-embed="pullquote" data-lazy-id="P0-27" data-node-id="78" class="embed"><blockquote data-theme-key="pullquote" class="css-1eiql25 e1pe3zr91"><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-before" class="css-5esr6z eagam8p0"></span><blockquote class="css-xw57yn e1pe3zr90">Ted knew he’d be better: now he’d seen the pitchers, he knew he could do it. </blockquote><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-after" class="css-0 eagam8p1"></span></blockquote></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="79" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Writers worked the clubhouse, trying to <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="79.1">explain</em> the Kid. Big Jimmy Foxx, a hero to Ted, said: &quot;Aw, he's just bein' a spoiled boy.&quot; The great Lefty Grove said if Williams didn't hustle, he'd punch him in the nose. Of course, all that made the papers. Now when writers came to his locker, Ted didn't wait for questions. &quot;HEY, WHAT STINKS?&quot; he'd yell in their faces. &quot;HEY! SOMETHING STINK IN HERE? OH, IT'S YOU, WELL, NO WONDER WITH THAT SHIT YOU WROTE.&quot; So, they made new nicknames for him: Terrible Ted, the Screwball, the Problem Child. Fans picked it up and gave him hell. It didn't seem to matter what he <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="79.3">did</em> anymore. And Ted read the stories in his hotel room and knew he was alone. Sure, he read the papers, though he always said he didn't. He read the stories twenty times, he'd recite them word for word. He'd pace the room and seethe, want to shut them up, want to hit them back. But he didn't know how.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="80" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">And Ted would sit alone in the locker room, boning his bats, not just the handle, like other guys did, but the whole bat, grinding down the wood, compressing the fiber tighter, making it tougher, harder, tighter. He would string the ball, he'd show them. He'd shut them up. Jesus, he was trying. And he was hitting. Wasn't his average up? Wasn't he leading the league in runs? He was doing it like he'd taught himself, like he'd dreamed. Wasn't that enough? What the hell did they want him to be?</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="81" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">What else could he be? Some players tried to help, to ease him up a bit. Once, Ted gave Doc Cramer a ride, and they were talking hitting, as Ted always did. It was at Kenmore Square that Cramer said: &quot;You know who's the best, don't you? You know who's the best in the league? You are.&quot; And Ted never forgot those words. But neither could he forget what was written, just as he couldn't forget one boo, just as he'd never forget the curve that struck him out a year before. Why didn't they understand? He could never forget.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="82" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">And one day he made an error, and then struck out, and it sounded like all of Fenway was booing, and he ran to the bench with his head down, the red rising in his face, the shame in his belly, and the rage. Ted thought: These are the ones who cheered, the fans I waved my cap to? Well, never again. He vowed to himself: Never again. And he could not forget that either.</p><hr data-node-id="83" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="84" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="84.0">Lou is in a Miami hospital</strong> for heart tests. Ted says I can drive up with him. He figures we'll talk, and he'll have me out of his hair. We start from his house and I wait for him on the porch, where a weary woman irons. The woman is filling in for Lou and she's been ironing for hours. Ted may wear a T-shirt until it's half holes and no color at all, but he wants it just so. The woman casts a look of despair at the pile and announces: &quot;She irons his <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="84.2">underpants</em>.&quot;</p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-28" data-node-id="85" class="embed"><div size="large" data-embed="body-image" class="align-center size-large embed css-uhpotl e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="1941 boston red sox" title="1941 Boston Red Sox" loading="lazy" width="6060" height="3449" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/portrait-of-members-of-the-boston-red-sox-baseball-team-as-news-photo-1662043213.jpg?resize=640:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/portrait-of-members-of-the-boston-red-sox-baseball-team-as-news-photo-1662043213.jpg?resize=980:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/portrait-of-members-of-the-boston-red-sox-baseball-team-as-news-photo-1662043213.jpg?resize=2048:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/portrait-of-members-of-the-boston-red-sox-baseball-team-as-news-photo-1662043213.jpg?resize=2048:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-1gccgwy e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Transcendental Graphics</span><span class="css-1r6mz5z e1geg53v0">//</span><span class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v1">Getty Images</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">The Boston Red Sox, 1941. </div></div></div></div></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="86" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted blows through the back door and makes for the car, Lou's Ford, which he proclaims &quot;a honey of a little car, boys!&quot; When Ted puts his seal of judgment on a thing or person, by habit he alerts the whole dugout. We are out of Islamorada on the crowded highway, U.S. 1, the only road that perseveres to these islets off the corner of the country, when Ted springs his key question. &quot;You read the book? Awright. Now we're going to see how smart YOU are. What would YOU do to start, I mean, the first goddamn thing now, the first thing you see when you're sitting in the seats and the lights go off, how would YOU start the movie?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="87" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted is considering a film deal for <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="87.1">My Turn at Bat</em>. He is working the topic of moviedom, as he does anything he wants to know. Now as he pilots the Ford through Key Largo, he listens with a grave frown to some possible first scenes. &quot;Awright. Now I'll tell you how it's supposed to start, I mean how the guy's doing it said....It's in a fighter plane, see, flying, from the pilot's eye, over KOREA, Seoul. And it's flying, slow and sunny and then <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="87.3">bang</em> WHAM <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="87.5">BOOOOMMM the biggest goddamn explosion ever on the screen</em>. I mean BOOOOOMMM. And the screen goes dark. DARK. For maybe ten seconds there's NOTHING. <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="87.7">NOTHING</em>. And then when it comes back there's the ball park and the crowd ROARING... and that's the beginning.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="88" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Sounds great, Ted.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="89" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Does it? LOOK IT THIS NOW. I wonder where he's goin'. Well, okay, he's gonna do <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="89.1">that</em>. Well, okay -- I'm passing, too. Fuck it.&quot; Ted is pushing traffic hard to be at the hospital by 2:00, when Lou's doctors have promised results from the heart tests. He is trying to be helpful, but he'd edgy.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="90" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;How long have you and Lou been together?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="91" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Oh, I've known Lou for thirty-five years. You shouldn't put any of that shit in there. Say I have a wonderful friend, that's all.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="92" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Yeah, but it makes a difference in how a man lives, Ted, whether he's got a woman or not -- &quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="93" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Boy, that Sylvester Stallone, he's really made something out of that Rocky, hasn't he?....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="94" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;So Ted, let me ask you what -- &quot;</p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-29" data-node-id="95" class="embed"><div size="large" data-embed="body-image" class="align-left size-large embed css-9hzlm7 e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="esquire covers" title="esquire covers" loading="lazy" width="864" height="1192" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/screen-shot-2022-09-01-at-10-41-03-am-1662043281.png?resize=320:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/screen-shot-2022-09-01-at-10-41-03-am-1662043281.png?resize=480:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/screen-shot-2022-09-01-at-10-41-03-am-1662043281.png?resize=480:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/screen-shot-2022-09-01-at-10-41-03-am-1662043281.png?resize=480:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-78jldq e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Esquire</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">The June 1986 cover of Esquire, the issue in which this story originally ran. </div></div></div></div></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="96" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;LOOK, I don't wanta go through my personal life with YOU, for Christ's sake. I won't talk to you about Lou, I won't talk to you about any of it. You came down here and you're talkin' about me, as I'm supposed to be different and all that....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="97" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Do you think you're different?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="98" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;NO, not a damn bit. I'm in a little bit different POSITION. I mean, I've had things happen to me that have, uh, made it possible for me to be different. DAMN DIFFERENT in some ways. Everybody's not a big league ballplayer, everybody doesn't have, uh, coupla hitches in the service, everybody hasn't had, uh, as much notoriety about 'em as I had ALL MY LIFE, so....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="99" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;So....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="100" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;I wanna go NORTH. I'm gonna go up here and go farther down. I made a mistake there, GODDAMNIT, HOW THE HELL DO I GET ON THE FUCKIN' THING? I'll make a U-turn....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="101" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Ted, I think you were more serious about living life on your own terms....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="102" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Well, I wanted to be alone at times. It was the hustle and the bustle of the crowd for seven months a year. So sure, I wanted a little more privacy, a little more quiet, a little more tranquility. This is the fucking left we wanted.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="103" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Yeah, but it's not just privacy, Ted. I'm not trying to make it seem unnatural. But what you toss <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="103.1">off</em> as a little more privacy led you off the continent, so far off in a corner that -- &quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="104" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Well, lemme tell you about Koufax. He got through playin' baseball, he went to a fuckin' little shitty remote town in Maine, and that's where he was for five years. Everybody thought he was a recluse, he wasn't very popular just 'cause he wanted to be alone and he finally moved out. Lemme tell you about Sterling Hayward, Hayden. HELL of an actor. And still he wanted to be ALONE, he wanted to TRAVEL, he wanted to be on his BOAT GOIN' TO THE SOUTH SEAS. So, see, that's not way outa line!....I guess I'll take a right, that oughta do it. Eight seventy-four, do you see 874 anyplace? Go down here till I get to Gilliam Road, or some goddamn thing....Fuck, 874's where I wanted to go, but looked like it was puttin' me back on this fuckin' turnpike, shit. So, you know, seeking privacy and, uh, seeking that kind of thing...what road is this?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="105" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;We're on Killian....So privacy, you don't think that's what?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="106" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;<em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="106.1">Unusual</em>, for Christ's sake. Shit.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="107" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;I don't think it's unusual either.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="108" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;WELL, YOU'RE MAKIN' A PROJECT OUT OF IT!&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="109" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;No, I don't think it's unusual....You don't think you're exceptionally combative?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="110" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Nahh, me? Not a bit. Hell, no. THAT SAY KENDALL? Does it? Well, I made a hell of a move here. HELL of a move! See, 874 is right off there, hospital's down here....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="111" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;You're a half-hour early, too.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="112" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Here it is, right here, too. Best hospital in Miami. Expensive sonofabitch, boy. Christ. I'm all for Medicare. And I've always thought that, ALWAYS thought that. Shit. WELL, WHERE ARE YOU GOING? Where ARE you going, lady? <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="112.1">Cunt</em>!&quot; Ted takes the parking space vacated by the lady and tells me he'll be back in an hour.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="113" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">When he comes back he has good news about Lou: all tests are negative, her heart is fine. &quot;Gee, I met the big cardiovascular man, he came in and I met him.&quot; Ted sounds twenty years younger.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="114" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He's walking to the car when a nurse passes. &quot;GEE, WASN'T IT A SHAME,&quot; Ted suddenly booms, &quot;THAT ALLIGATOR BIT THAT LITTLE GIRL'S LEG OFF?&quot; He casts a sly sideward glance at the nurse to see if she's fallen for his favorite joke.<br data-node-id="114.1"/></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="115" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Honey of a little shittin' car!&quot; he sings out as we hit the road. Now there is no fretting with traffic. Ted makes all the turns. Along the way, he sings forth a monologue about cars, this car, this road, this town of Homestead, that house, his house, the new house he's planning in central Florida, up on a hill, just about the highest point in the whole goddamn sate, what a deal he's getting there, Citrus Hills, HELL of a deal; about his hopes for his kids, his daughter, Claudia, only fourteen, who lives in Vermont with her mother, Ted's third wife, who was too much of a pain in the ass to live with, but gee, she's done a hell of a job with those kids, HELL of a job, the little girl is an actress, she had the lead in the Christmas play and she was so good, the papers up there all said she bears watching, SHE BEARS WATCHING, and her brother, Ted's boy, John Henry, he's picking colleges now, he's a good boy and Ted's critical, but he can't see too much wrong with that boy, and even the big daughter, Bobby Jo, she's thirty-eight already, still can bust Ted's chops pretty good, boys, but she's straightening out now; and these islands, there's bonefish here, used to be wonderful, years ago, there was NOTHING, NOTHING except a few of the best fishermen God ever made, and a narrow road between bay and sea, just a little shittin' road, and some women who weren't half bad on the water of off it either, and the world here was empty and the water was clear and you could have a few pops of rum, maybe get a little horny, go see friends, that's all there was here, a few friends, thirty, thirty-five years ago, when this place was young, when he first fished with Jimmy and he met Lou....</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="116" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Gee, I'm so fuckin' happy about Louise,&quot; Ted says. &quot;Goddamn, she's a great person. Have more fun with her than...Goddamn.&quot; </p><hr data-node-id="117" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="118" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="118.0">They booed in Boston?</strong> Well, not in Detroit, the 1941 All-Star Game, with all the nation listening in. Ted doubled in a run in the fourth, but the National League still led 5-3, going into the ninth. Then an infield hit, a single, a walk, a botched double play, and here it was: two out, two on, bottom of the ninth. <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="118.2">Here's the Great Ted Williams</em>. Claude Passeau, the Cubbie on the mound, sends a mean fastball in on his fists. <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="118.4">Williams swings!</em> When the ball made the seats, Ted started jumping on the base path. DiMaggio met him at home plate, Bob Feller ran out in street clothes, Cronin jumped the box-seat rail, the dugout emptied. The manager, Del Baker, kissed him on the forehead. They carried the Kid off the field. </p><section data-embed="pullquote" data-lazy-id="P0-30" data-node-id="119" class="embed"><blockquote data-theme-key="pullquote" class="css-1eiql25 e1pe3zr91"><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-before" class="css-5esr6z eagam8p0"></span><blockquote class="css-xw57yn e1pe3zr90">There was no rookie of the year award, but Babe Ruth himself put the title on Ted, and that seemed good enough.</blockquote><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-after" class="css-0 eagam8p1"></span></blockquote></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="120" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He was showing them all now; after the All-Star break, Ted was still hitting more than .400. Sure, guys hit like that for a month, but then tailed off. No one in the league hit like that for a year, not since the Twenties, and each day the whole country watched. Writers from New York joined the Sox. <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="120.1">Life</em> brought its new strobe-light camera to photograph Ted in his shorts, swinging like he did in front of the mirror. Ted was on national radio: &quot;Can you keep it up, Kid?&quot; It was murderous pressure. By September, he was slipping, almost a point a day. On the last day, the Sox would have two games in Philadelphia. Ted had slipped to .39955. The way they round off averages, that's still .400. Cronin came to Ted on the eve of the twin bill and offered: &quot;You could sit it out, Kid, have it made.&quot; But Ted said he'd play.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="121" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">That night, he and Johnny Orlando walked Philadelphia. Ted stopped for milk shakes, Johnny for whiskey. Ten thousand people came to Shibe Park, though the games meant nothing. Connie Mack, the dour and penurious owner of the A's, threatened his men with fines if they eased up on Williams. But Ted didn't need help. First game, he got a single, then a home run, then two more singles. Second game, two more hits: one a screaming double that hit Mr. Mack's right-field loudspeaker so hard that the old man had to buy a new horn. In all, Ted went six-for-eight, and .406 for his third season. That night, he went out, he went out for chocolate ice cream. </p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="122" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Who could tell what he'd do the next year: maybe .450, the best <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="122.1">ever</em>, or break the Babe's record of sixty homers. He got a contract for $30,000, and he meant to fix up his mother's house. He'd have more money than he'd ever expected. He was the toast of the nation. But then the nation went to war.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="123" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted wanted to play. He'd read where some admiral said we'd kick the Japs back to Tokyo in six months. What was that compared to hitting? A lawyer in Minnesota drew up a plea for deferment, and Ted okayed the request: he was entitled, as his mother's support. When the local board refused deferment, the lawyer sent it up for review by the presidential board. That's when the papers got it. In headlines the size of howitzer shells, they said Ted didn't want to fight for his country. Teddy Ballgame just wanted to play.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="124" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Tom Yawkey called to say he could be making the mistake of his life. The league president told Ted to go ahead and play. Papers ran man-on-the-streets polls. In Boston, Ted was bigger news than war in the Pacific. At spring training, Joe Cronin said he'd be on his own with fans. &quot;To hell with them,&quot; Ted spat. &quot;I've heard plenty of boos.&quot; Still, he remembered the venomous letters that said he was an ingrate or a traitor. The one that hurt the most said nothing at all: it was just a blank sheet of paper, <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="124.1">yellow</em> paper.</p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-31" data-node-id="125" class="embed"><div size="large" data-embed="body-image" class="align-center size-large embed css-uhpotl e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="ted williams swinging bat" title="Ted Williams Swinging Bat" loading="lazy" width="3566" height="2542" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-boston-red-sox-news-photo-1662043472.jpg?resize=640:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-boston-red-sox-news-photo-1662043472.jpg?resize=980:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-boston-red-sox-news-photo-1662043472.jpg?resize=2048:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-boston-red-sox-news-photo-1662043472.jpg?resize=2048:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-1gccgwy e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Bettmann</span><span class="css-1r6mz5z e1geg53v0">//</span><span class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v1">Getty Images</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">Williams swinging at bat, 1941. </div></div></div></div></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="126" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Opening day in Boston, reporters sat in the left-field stands, out there with soldiers and sailors, to record reaction to Ted. The Kid treated the day as a personal challenge. His first time up, two on, two strikes, he got a waist-high fastball and drilled it into the bleachers. All the fans rose to cheer, servicemen among them. The Kid was back, and Fenway was with him. &quot;Yeah, 98 percent were for me,&quot; Ted said later, as he scraped his bat. A writer said: &quot;You mean 100 percent. I didn't hear a boo.&quot; Ted said: &quot;Yeah, they were for me, except a couple of kids in the left-field stand, and a guy out in right. I could hear them.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="127" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">In May, he enlisted for Navy wings and that shut up most of the hecklers. Still, he was always in a stew of contempt for some joker who said something unfair. It seemed Ted courted the rage now, used it to bone his own fiber. Now there was no awkwardness, no blushing before he blew. It was automatic, a switch in his gut that snapped on and then, watch out for the Kid. One day in July, a fan in left was riding Ted pretty hard. Ted came to bat in the fifth: he took a strange stance and swung late, hit a line drive, but well foul into the left-field seats. Next pitch, again he swung late, hit another liner, but this stayed fair -- and Ted didn't run, barely made it to second. Cronin yanked him out of the game, fined him $250 for loafing. But Ted wasn't loafing, the hit caught him by surprise. He's been trying to kill the heckler with a line drive foul.</p><hr data-node-id="128" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="129" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="129.0">Ted loved the service,</strong> its certainty and ease. He never had a problem with authority. It was drawing his own lines that gave him fits. He had his fears about the mathematics, navigation problems, and instrument work. But at Amherst College, where the Navy started training, he found his mind was able, and he was pleased. And he loved the feel of an airplane. He was good, right from the start. There was coordination in it, and care: those were natural to him. And he was a constant student, always learning in the air. But he was proudest of his gunnery, the way he could hold back until the last pass, then pour out the lead and shred the sleeve. That wasn't study, that was art. He got his wings near the top of his class and signed on as an instructor at Pensacola, Florida. He was happy, and good at his job. Strangely, in uniform, he was freer than before. </p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="130" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">On the day he was commissioned (second lieutenant, U.S. Marines), he married that daughter of the hunting guide, Doris Soule from Minnesota. Now, for the first time, he'd have a house, a place on the coast near the base. And now, on off days, he'd scrape up some gas stamps, grab his fly rod, find a lonesome canal, and lose himself in a hunt for snook. But back at the base, Ted would grab a cadet and take him up in his SNJ, and the new guy of course was goggle-eyed, flying with <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="130.1">Ted Williams</em>, and Ted would make his plane dance over the coast, then he'd dive and point, and yell to the cadet: <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="130.3">&quot;That's where the Kid fished yesterday.&quot;</em></p><section data-embed="pullquote" data-lazy-id="P0-32" data-node-id="131" class="embed"><blockquote data-theme-key="pullquote" class="css-1eiql25 e1pe3zr91"><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-before" class="css-5esr6z eagam8p0"></span><blockquote class="css-xw57yn e1pe3zr90">He was happy, and good at his job. Strangely, in uniform, he was freer than before.</blockquote><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-after" class="css-0 eagam8p1"></span></blockquote></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="132" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60"><em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="132.0"><br data-node-id="132.0.0"/></em></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="133" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Orders came through slowly for him. What base commander would give him up as ornament and outfielder? At last he got combat training and packed up for the Pacific. But Ted was just getting to Hawaii when Japan folded. So he packed up again for Boston, and now he felt he was going to war.</p><hr data-node-id="134" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="135" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="135.0">He came back like</strong> he owned the game. Opening day, Washington, after a three-year layoff: <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="135.2">crack</em>, a four-hundred-foot home run. And then another and another, all around the league. By the All-Star break in '46, he was hitting .365, with twenty-seven home runs. In the All-Star Game, Ted alone ruined the National League: four straight hits, two homers, and five runs batted in.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="136" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">And the Red Sox were burying the American League. Tom Yawkey's millions were paying off. The team as a whole was hitting .300, and Ted was hammering the right-field walls. In the first game of two in Cleveland, he hit three homers, one a grand slam when the Sox were behind, the second with two on to tie, the third in the bottom of the ninth to win 11-10.
