As Ted came up in the second game, Cleveland's manager, Lou Boudreau, started moving men: the right fielder backed toward the corner, center fielder played the wall in right-center; the third baseman moved behind second, and Boudreau, the shortstop, played a deep second base; the second baseman stood in short right, the first baseman stood behind his bag. There were eight men on one half of the field (the left fielder was alone on the other) and Ted stood at home plate and laughed out loud. </p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="137" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">There never had been anything like it. He had bent the nature of the game. But he would not bend his own, and slap the ball for singles to left. He hit into the teeth of the Shift (soon copied around the league), and when he slumped, and the Sox with him, the papers started hammering Ted again, his pride, his &quot;attitude.&quot; At last, against the Shift in Cleveland, Ted sliced a drive to left-center, and slid across the plate with an inside-the-park home run, first and last of his career. The Sox had their first pennant since 1918. But the headlines didn't say, SOX CLINCH. Instead, eight-column banners cried that Ted stayed away from the champagne party. &quot;Ted Williams,&quot; Dave Egan wrote in the <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="137.1">Record</em>, &quot;is not a team man.&quot; And when St. Louis pulled the Shift in the Series and held Ted to singles, five-for-twenty-five, a new banner read: WILLIAMS BUNTS. And the Red Sox lost the Series, first and last of his career, and after the seventh game, in St. Louis, Ted went to the train, closed his compartment, hung his head, and cried. When he looked up, he saw a crowd watching him through the window. The papers wrote: &quot;Ted Williams cannot win the big ones.&quot; The Associated Press voted him number two in a poll for Flop of the Year.</p><hr data-node-id="138" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="139" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="139.0">It seemed like Ted couldn't</strong> laugh anymore, not in a ball park. He said he was going to Florida to fish. He didn't want to see a bat for months. Soon that was a pattern: one year, before spring training, he tucked in a week in the Everglades. Next year, it was a month. Year after that, longer. In early 1948, the papers discovered that Doris was in a Boston hospital to deliver Ted's first child. But where was the big guy? In Florida? FISHING? The mothers of Boston pelted the press with angry letters. &quot;To hell with them,&quot; Ted said. He didn't come north for two days. And two days later, he was back fishing. In two years, he'd moved Doris and his daughter, Barbara Joyce, to a house in Miami, the first he'd ever owned. Hut he never stayed home there either. He heard about some men in the Keys catching bonefish with light fly tackle. When Ted tried this new sport, he found a love that would last longer than any of his marriages. </p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-33" data-node-id="140" class="embed"><div size="large" data-embed="body-image" class="align-center size-large embed css-uhpotl e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="ted williams" title="Ted Williams" loading="lazy" width="3072" height="2048" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/baseball-hall-of-famer-ted-williams-who-has-suffered-3-news-photo-1661946602.jpg?resize=640:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/baseball-hall-of-famer-ted-williams-who-has-suffered-3-news-photo-1661946602.jpg?resize=980:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/baseball-hall-of-famer-ted-williams-who-has-suffered-3-news-photo-1661946602.jpg?resize=2048:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/baseball-hall-of-famer-ted-williams-who-has-suffered-3-news-photo-1661946602.jpg?resize=2048:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-1gccgwy e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Thomas S England</span><span class="css-1r6mz5z e1geg53v0">//</span><span class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v1">Getty Images</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">Williams in Florida, 1994.</div></div></div></div></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="141" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">The Keys were empty, their railroad wrecked by a hurricane in 1935. There were only a few thousand souls on one road that ran for a hundred miles; the rest was just mangrove and mosquitoes, crushed coral islands, and shining water. In Islamorada -- a town of one store, a bar, a restaurant, one gas pump -- a few fishing guides, led by Jimmy Albright, were poling their skiffs over shallows that only they knew, hunting bonefish and inventing an art as they went along. These were Ted's kind of men, who'd sneer or scream at a chairman of the stock exchange if he made a lousy cast. Islamorada was a strange meritocracy: if you could not play a fish, tie a fly, cast a line through the wind, you were no one in this town.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="142" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted could do it all, brilliantly. The guides didn't make much fuss about his fame, but they loved his fishing. His meticulous detail work, always an oddity at Fenway Park, was respected here as the mark of a fine angler. Ted had the best tackle, the best reels, best rods, the perfect line, his lures were impeccable. He'd work for hours at a bench in his house, implanting balsa plugs with lead so they'd sail off a spinning rod just so, then settle in the water slowly like fly. He could stand on the bow of a skiff all day, watching the water for signs of fish, and soon he was seeing them before the guides. His casts were quick and long, his power was immense. He never seemed to snap a line, never tangled up, his knots were sure, his knowledge grew, and he always wanted to know <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="142.1">more</em>. He'd question Jimmy relentlessly and argue every point. But if you showed him something once, he never needed showing again. He fished with Jimmy week after week, and one afternoon as he stood on the bow, he asked without turning his head: &quot;Who's the best you ever fished?&quot; Jimmy said a name, Al Mathers. Ted nodded, &quot;Uh-huh,&quot; and asked another question, but he vowed to himself: &quot;He don't know it yet, but the best angler he's had is me.&quot;</p><hr data-node-id="143" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="144" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="144.0">Every winter,</strong> he'd fish the flats, then head north to make his appearance at the Boston Sportsmen's Show. He'd spend a few days doing fly-casting stunts and then take a couple of hours, at most, to tell Tom Yawkey what he wanted for a contract. His salary was enormous. He was the first to break Babe Ruth's $80,000. Ted didn't care for the money as much as the record. It was history now that was the burr on his back. The joy was gone, but not the dream.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="145" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Every day, every season, he was still first to the ball park, where he'd strip to shorts and bone his bats; still first out to the cage, where he'd bark his imaginary play-by-play: &quot;Awright, Detroit, top of the ninth...&quot; Then back to his locker for a clean shirt and up at a trot to the dugout, to clap a hostile eye on the pitcher warming up, to pick apart his delivery, hunting for any weakness. No, Ted would not give up on one game, one time at bat, a single pitch. No one since Ruth had hit so many home runs per times at bat. No one in the league hit like Ted, year after year: .342, .343, .369, .343.... It seemed he never broke a bat at the plate, but he broke a hundred in the clubhouse runway. If he failed at the plate he'd scream at himself, &quot;YOU GODDAMN FOOL!&quot; and bash the cement, while the Sox in the dugout stared ahead with mute smiles. Once, after a third strike, he smashed the water pipe to the cooler with his bare fists. No could believe it until the flood began. And on each opening day, Ted would listen to the national anthem and he'd feel the hair rise on the back of his neck, and his bands would clench, and he'd vow to himself: &quot;This year, the best <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="145.1">ever</em>.&quot;</p><section data-embed="pullquote" data-lazy-id="P0-34" data-node-id="146" class="embed"><blockquote data-theme-key="pullquote" class="css-1eiql25 e1pe3zr91"><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-before" class="css-5esr6z eagam8p0"></span><blockquote class="css-xw57yn e1pe3zr90">Ted didn't care for the money as much as the record. It was history now that was the burr on his back. The joy was gone, but not the dream.</blockquote><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-after" class="css-0 eagam8p1"></span></blockquote></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="147" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">In the 1950 All-Star Game, he crashed the outfield wall to catch a drive by Ralph Kiner. His elbow was broken, with thirteen clips off the radius. Surgeons thought he was through, but Ted returned in two months. His first game back, once again: home run, and four-for-four. But Ted could tell as weeks went by that the elbow was not the same. The ball didn't jump off his bat. So all next winter, Ted stayed in the Keys, where he poled a skiff, hunting bonefish and rebuilding his arm. He was pushing thirty-three now, just coming to know how short was his time. But then, after the '51 season, he was called back to the Marines, drafted for a two-year hitch in Korea. It seemed like his time was up.  </p><hr data-node-id="148" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="149" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="149.0">Ted’s living room has a wide</strong> white armchair, into and out of which he heaves himself twenty times a day; the chair has a wide white ottoman onto which he'll flop, as whim dictates, one or both of his big legs. From this chair, he roars commands and inquiries, administering the house and grounds. Across the room, a big TV shows his <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="149.2">National Geographic</em> specials. At his side, a table holds his reading correspondence. At the moment, these piles are topped by <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="149.4">Yeager: An Autobiography</em>, and teachers' reports on his son, John Henry. To Ted's right, ten feet away, there's a doorway to the kitchen, through which Lou can supply him and let him know who that was on the phone. To his left and behind, a grand window affords a view of a patio, his dock, some mangrove, and some Florida Bay. Finally, ahead and to the right, in a distant semicircle, there are chairs and a couch for visitors.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="150" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;NOW WE'RE GONNA SEE HOW MUCH <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="150.1">YOU</em> KNOW, SONOFABITCH,&quot; Ted is shouting at Jack Brothers. Jimmy Albright is there, too. The shouting is ritual. </p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="151" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Ru-mer. R-U-M-E-R.&quot; Brothers contends he is spelling the name of the first spinning wheel. But Ted had hurled himself up to fetch a fishing encyclopedia, and now he's back in the chair, digging through to the section on spinning. Just so things don't get dull, he says: &quot;Where'd you get that HAIRCUT? D'you have to PAY FOR IT?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="152" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted and Jimmy began this colloquy in the early Truman years. Jack helped heat it up when he drifted down from Brooklyn a few years after the war, before Islamorada got its second restaurant or first motel, not to mention the other ten motels, the condos, gift shops, Burger King, or the billboard to proclaim this place: SPORTFISHING CAPITAL OF THE WORLD. These elders are responsible for a lot of history here, as they helped create flats fishing and turn it into a sport/industry (which they not quietly deplore). Jimmy and Jack were teachers of the first generation of saltwater anglers. Ted is the star of that generation, and its most ferocious pupil. </p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-35" data-node-id="153" class="embed"><div size="large" data-embed="body-image" class="align-right size-large embed css-f5ml56 e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="ted williams" title="Spring Training - Boston Red Sox" loading="lazy" width="3000" height="3600" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/outfielder-ted-williams-of-the-boston-red-sox-poses-for-an-news-photo-1662043803.jpg?resize=320:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/outfielder-ted-williams-of-the-boston-red-sox-poses-for-an-news-photo-1662043803.jpg?resize=480:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/outfielder-ted-williams-of-the-boston-red-sox-poses-for-an-news-photo-1662043803.jpg?resize=480:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/outfielder-ted-williams-of-the-boston-red-sox-poses-for-an-news-photo-1662043803.jpg?resize=480:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-78jldq e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Diamond Images</span><span class="css-1r6mz5z e1geg53v0">//</span><span class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v1">Getty Images</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">Williams in Spring training, 1950. </div></div></div></div></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="154" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Here. HERE! 'Mr. Brown began importing SPINNERS, starting with the LUXAR....' THE LUXAR, WANNA SEE? GO AHEAD, SONOFABITCH!&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="155" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Yeah, but that don't say the first spinning reel <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="155.1">manufactured</em>.&quot; Brothers grins in triumph. &quot;Sonofabitch, with your books!&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="156" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;This is the goddamn HISTORY, Brothers. Not a FUCKING THING about RUMOR, RHEUMER, RHOOOMAN...I GUESS YOU DIDN'T KNOW MUCH ABOUT SPINNING REELS, DID YOU?&quot;<br data-node-id="156.1"/></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="157" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted is always the one with the books. He wants <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="157.1">answers</em>, not a lot of bullshit. Ted is always reading history, biography, fact of all kinds. He doesn't like much made of this, as he's tender on the subject of his education. Once in a camp in Africa, while he and his coauthor, John Underwood, gazed at the night sky, Ted turned from the stars and sighed: &quot;Jeez, I wish I was smart like you.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="158" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Now he reports to his friends on his college tours with his son, John Henry: &quot;So we get to Babson and I like it. Babson's a pretty good school, boys. HELL of a school, but, uh, they got dorms, boys and girls all in one dorm, see, and I look on the walls and they're written all over, Fuck this and Fuck that, I'm thinking, Gee, right out there on the walls. It just seemed, you know...&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="159" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Liberal?&quot; Jimmy suggests.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="160" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Well, I like to see a place with a little more standards than <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="160.1">that</em>. So we get to Bates. We got this German girl to show us around, see? And she was a smart little shit, two languages, and she's telling us what she's studying, <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="160.3">aw</em>, a smart little shit! She give us the tour, see, and John Henry loved Bates, LOVED it. We get back to the office and she goes out. I don't know, she musta told someone, told some of her friends, who she just showed around, see? Then somebody <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="160.5">told</em> her. She didn't know, see....</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="161" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Well, a minute later, she's back with some kid and he says, OH, Mr. Williams! And OH this and OH that. And <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="161.1">then</em> we start talking. And how about <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="161.3">this</em>, how about <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="161.5">that</em>, and how would John Henry like to come for a <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="161.7">weekend</em>, get the feel of the place, you know....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="162" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted stops for a moment and thinks to himself. He doesn't really have to finish the thought for his friends, who can see him beaming in his big chair. So he just trails off, to himself:</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="163" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;...boy mighta thought the old man wasn't gonna...you know, around a college....Well!&quot;</p><hr data-node-id="164" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="165" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="165.0">The mayor and the Red Sox</strong> held a day for Ted when he left for flight school. Three weeks into the '52 season, at Fenway, they gave him a Cadillac, and made a donation to the Jimmy Fund, a charity for sick children that Ted supported. They gave him a <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="165.2">Ted Williams Memory Book</em>, with signatures of four hundred thousand fans. For his last at bat, bottom of the seventh, he gave them a three-run homer to win the game 5-3. He threw a party that night, at his Boston hotel. The crowd was mostly cooks and firemen, bellhops, cabbies, ice cream men. Ted never liked a smart crowd. Smart people too often asked: &quot;Oh, was your father a ballplayer?&quot; &quot;Oh, what did your mother do?&quot; Ted didn't like to talk about that.</p><section data-embed="pullquote" data-lazy-id="P0-36" data-node-id="166" class="embed"><blockquote data-theme-key="pullquote" class="css-1eiql25 e1pe3zr91"><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-before" class="css-5esr6z eagam8p0"></span><blockquote class="css-xw57yn e1pe3zr90">This is the goddamn HISTORY, Brothers. Not a FUCKING THING about RUMOR, RHEUMER, RHOOOMAN...</blockquote><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-after" class="css-0 eagam8p1"></span></blockquote></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="167" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He was just Captain Williams, U.S. Marines, at his flight base at Pohang, Korea. He had a shed for a home and a cot with inner-tube strips for springs. The base was a sea of mud, the air was misty and cold, and he was always sick. He was flying close air support, low strafing, and bombing runs. His plane was a jet now, an F-9 Panther, but he couldn't take much joy from flying. He was in and out of sick bay. Doctors called it a virus, then pneumonia, but his squadron was short of pilots, so he always flew. </p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="168" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">On a bombing run, north of the 38th parallel, Ted lost sight of the plane ahead. He dropped through clouds, and when he came out, he was much too low. North Koreans sent up a hail of bullets. Ted's place was hit and set afire. The stick stiffened and shook in his hand; his hydraulics were gone. Every warning light was red. The radio quit. A Marine in a nearby F-9 was pointing wildly at Ted's place. He was trying to signal: &quot;Fire! Bail out!&quot; But Ted's biggest fear was ejecting; at six three, wedged in as he was, he'd leave his kneecaps under his gauges. So the other pilot led him to a base. Ted hauled his plane into a turn and he felt a shudder of explosion. One of his wheel doors had blown out. Now he was burning below, too. He made for a runway with fire streaming thirty feet behind. Koreans in a village saw his plane and ran for their lives. Only one wheel came down; he had no dive breaks, air flaps, nothing to slow the plane. He hit the concrete at 225 miles per hour and slid for almost a mile, while he mashed the useless brakes and screamed, &quot;STOP YOU DIRTY SONOFABITCH STOP STOP STOP.&quot; When the F-9 stopped skidding, he somersaulted out the hatch and slammed his helmet to the ground. Two Marines grabbed him on the tarmac, and walked him away as the plane burned to char. </p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="169" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He was flying the next day, and day after. There weren't enough pilots to rest a man. Ted was sicker, weak and gaunt. Soon his ears were so bad he couldn't hear the radio. He had flown thirty-seven missions and won three air medals when they sent him to a hospital ship. Doctors sent him to Hawaii and then to Bethesda, Maryland, where at last they gave him a discharge. His thirty-fifth birthday was coming up, he was tired and ill. He didn't want to do anything, much less suit up to play. But Ford Frick, the commissioner, asked him to the '53 All-Star Game, just to throw out the first ball.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="170" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">So Ted went to Cincinnati, sat in a sport coat in the dugout. Players greeted him like a lost brother; even Ted couldn't hear a boo in the stands. Tom Yawkey was there and Joe Cronin; they worked on the kid. The league president asked him to come back; the National League president, too. Branch Rickey sat him down for a talk; Casey Stengel put in a plea. Ted when to Bethesda to ask the doctors, and then he told the waiting press to send a message to the fans at Fenway: &quot;Warm up your lungs.&quot; He took ten days of batting practice and returned with the Red Sox to Boston. First game, Fenway Park, bottom of the seventh: pinch-hit home run. <br data-node-id="170.1"/></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="171" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted Williams was the greatest old hitter. In two months, upon return from Korea, he batted .407 and hit a home run once in every seven at bats. For the next two years, he led the league (.345 and .356), but injuries and walks robbed him of the titles: he didn't get the minimum four hundred at bats. In 1956, he lost the title in the season's last week to twenty-four-year-old Mickey Mantle (who finished .353 to Ted's .345). The next year, Mantle had an even better season, but Ted, at age thirty-nine, pulled away and won, at .388, more than twenty points ahead of Mantle, more than sixty points ahead of anyone else. With five more hits (say, the leg hits that a younger man would get), it would have been .400. As it was, it stood at the highest average since his own .406, sixteen years before. In 1958, Ted battled for the crown again, this time with a teammate, Pete Runnels. They were even in September, but then, once again, Ted pulled way to win at .328. For the final fifty-five games (including one on his fortieth birthday), he batted .403.</p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-37" data-node-id="172" class="embed"><div size="large" data-embed="body-image" class="align-center size-large embed css-uhpotl e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="captain ted williams in airplane cockpit" title="Captain Ted Williams in Airplane Cockpit" loading="lazy" width="3337" height="2590" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/marine-captain-ted-williams-a-former-boston-red-sox-slugger-news-photo-1662044081.jpg?resize=640:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/marine-captain-ted-williams-a-former-boston-red-sox-slugger-news-photo-1662044081.jpg?resize=980:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/marine-captain-ted-williams-a-former-boston-red-sox-slugger-news-photo-1662044081.jpg?resize=2048:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/marine-captain-ted-williams-a-former-boston-red-sox-slugger-news-photo-1662044081.jpg?resize=2048:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-1gccgwy e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Bettmann</span><span class="css-1r6mz5z e1geg53v0">//</span><span class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v1">Getty Images</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">Williams prepares for takeoff. </div></div></div></div></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="173" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He accomplished these prodigies despite troubles that would have made most men quit. In 1954, he made spring training for the first time in three years, but he wasn't on the field a minute before he fell and broke his collarbone. He was out six weeks and had steel bar wired into his clavicle. (First day back, twin bill in Detroit: two home runs, eight-for-nine, seven RBIs.) In 1955, Doris alleged in divorce court that he'd treated her with &quot;extreme cruelty&quot; and constant profane abuse. Boston papers ran the story under two-inch headlines: TED GETS DIVORCE, with a &quot;box score&quot; on the money, the house, the car, and &quot;Mrs. Ted's&quot; custody of Bobby Jo. In 1956, Ted came forth with his Great Expectorations. In a scoreless game with the Yankees, in front of Fenway's biggest crowd since World War II, he was booed for an error, and he let fans know what he thought of them: he spat toward the right-field stands and spat toward the left, and when fans rained more boos on his head, he leaped out of the dugout and sprayed all around. &quot;Oh, no, this is a bad scene,&quot; Curt Gowdy, the Sox broadcaster, mourned to his microphone. Tom Yawkey heard the game on the radio, and Ted got a $5,000 fine (tying another Babe Ruth record). Boston writers said Ted ought to quit. But Ted was in the next game, Family Night, and at his appearance, fans gave him a five-minute ovation. (He then hit a home run in the bottom of the eighth and clapped his hand over his mouth as he scored the winning run.) In 1957, grippe knocked him flat and stuck him in his hotel for seventeen days in September. He came back and hit four consecutive home runs. In 1958, ptomaine from bad oysters wrecked opening day, then he injured an ankle, pulled a muscle in his side, and hurt his wrist twice. In September, after a called third strike, Ted threw his bat and watched in horror as it sailed to the stands and clonked a gray-haired lady on the head. Ted sat in tears in the dugout and had to be ordered to his place in left field. But over the next twenty at-bats, he hit .500.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="174" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Now the switch in his gut was always on. The Red Sox gave him a single room and barred the press from the clubhouse for two hours before each game. But it wasn't outside annoyance that was fueling Ted's rage. He'd wake up in the middle of the night, screaming obscenities in the dark. He kept himself alone and pushed away affection. There were plenty of women who would have loved to help. But Ted would say: &quot;WOMEN?&quot; and then he'd grab his crotch. &quot;ALL THEY WANT IS WHAT I GOT RIGHT HERE.&quot; Now the press didn't cover just explosions on the field. The <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="174.1">American</em> wrote him up for shredding a telephone book all over the floor when a hotel maid failed to clean his room. &quot;Now tell me some more,&quot; wrote Austen Lake, &quot;about Ted's big, charitable, long-suffering spirit.&quot; Roger Kahn reported a scene when Ted was asked about Billy Klaus, the shortstop who was coming back after a bad year. &quot;You're asking ME about a BAD YEAR?...OLD T.S.W., HE DON'T HAVE BAD YEARS.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="175" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">But old Ted had a terrible year in 1959. A pain in his neck turned to stiffness, and he was in traction for three weeks. When he came out, he could barely look at the pitcher. He average languished below .300 for the first time in his career. For the first time, he was benched for not hitting. The sight of the Kid at plate was pathetic; even the papers softened. They started summing up his career, treating him like an old building menaced by the wrecking ball. He finished at .254 and went to Tom Yawkey. &quot;Why don't you just wrap it up?&quot; Yawkey said, and Ted started to boil. No one was going to make him retire. Ted said he meant to play, and Yawkey, who loved the Kid, offered to renew his contract: $125,000, the highest ever. No, Ted said, he'd had a lousy year and he wanted a cut. So Ted signed for $90,000 and came back one more time.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="176" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Opening day, Washington: A five-hundred-foot home run. Next day, another. He slammed his five-hundredth in Cleveland, passed Lou Gehrig and then Mel Ott. Only Foxx and Ruth would top him on the all-time list. At forty-two, Ted finished his year with twenty-nine homers and .316. Talk revived that Ted might be back. But this was really quits. On his last day at Fenway, a headline cried: WHAT WILL WE DO WITHOUT TED? And though the day was dreary and the season without hope, ten thousand came out to cheer him and hear him say goodbye. There was another check for the Jimmy Fund and, this time, a silver bowl. And Ted made a speech that said, despite all, he felt lucky to play for these fans. And when he came up in the eighth and they stood to cheer, he showed them what Ted Williams could do. He hit a Jack Fisher fastball into the bullpen in right field. And he thought about tipping his cap as he rounded first but he couldn't, even then, couldn't forget, so he ran it straight into the dugout, and wouldn't come out for a bow. </p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="177" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Now it was no hobby: Ted fished harder and fished more than any man around. After his divorce from Doris, he'd made his home in Islamorada, bought a little place on the ocean side, with no phone and just room for one man and gear. He'd wake before dawn and spend the day in his boat, then come in, maybe cook a steak, maybe drive off to a Cuban or Italian joint where they served big portions and left him alone. Then, back home, he'd tie a few flies and be in bed by 10:00. He kept it very spare. He didn't even have a TV. That's how he met Louise. He wanted to see a Joe Louis fight, so Jimmy took him to Lou's big house. Her husband was a businessman from Ohio, and they had a TV, they had everything. Lou had her five kids, the best home, best furniture, best car, and best guides. Though she wasn't a woman of leisure, she was a pretty good angler, too. She could talk fishing with Ted. Yes, they could talk. And soon, Lou would have a little money of her own, an inheritance that she'd use to buy a divorce. She wanted to do for herself, she said. And there was something else, too. &quot;I met Ted Williams,&quot; Louise said. &quot;And he was the most gorgeous thing I ever saw in my life.&quot;</p><section data-embed="body-image" data-lazy-id="P0-38" data-node-id="178" class="embed"><div size="large" data-embed="body-image" class="align-center size-large embed css-uhpotl e1fodxfw4"><div class="css-uwraif e1fodxfw3"><img draggable="true" alt="ted williams preparing flys for fishing" title="Ted Williams Preparing Flys for Fishing" loading="lazy" width="4014" height="3260" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:auto;" sizes="100vw" srcset="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-a-mighty-handy-man-with-a-bat-for-the-boston-news-photo-1662044151.jpg?resize=640:* 640w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-a-mighty-handy-man-with-a-bat-for-the-boston-news-photo-1662044151.jpg?resize=980:* 980w, https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-a-mighty-handy-man-with-a-bat-for-the-boston-news-photo-1662044151.jpg?resize=2048:* 1120w" src="https://hips.hearstapps.com/hmg-prod/images/ted-williams-a-mighty-handy-man-with-a-bat-for-the-boston-news-photo-1662044151.jpg?resize=2048:*" class="css-0 e1g79fud0"/><div class="css-1gccgwy e1fodxfw2"><figcaption data-theme-key="photo-credit-figcaption" class="css-1am3yn9 e1g9hcy40"><span data-theme-key="photo-credit-creditor" class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v2">Bettmann</span><span class="css-1r6mz5z e1geg53v0">//</span><span class="css-qt9nna e1geg53v1">Getty Images</span></figcaption><div data-element="caption" class="css-s3no6j e1fodxfw1">Williams prepares for a fishing trip, 1950. </div></div></div></div></section> <p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="180" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Now Ted's life was his to make, too. He signed a six-figure deal with Sears, to lend his name to their line of tackle, hunting gear, and sporting goods. Now, when Hurricane Donna wrecked his little house on the ocean, he bought his three-bedroom place on the bay, near Louise's house. Now he bought a salmon pool on the Miramichi, in New Brunswick, Canada, and he fished the summer season there. In Islamorada, he was out every day, fall, winter, spring. He wanted the most and biggest -- bonefish, tarpon, salmon -- he called then the Big Three. He wanted a thousand of each, and kept books on his progress. He thought fishing and talked fishing and taught fishing at shows for Sears. He felt the joy of the sport, still. But now there was something else: the switch that clicked when he'd get a hot fish that ran and broke off his lure: Ted would slam his rod to the deck, or break it in half on the boat. &quot;HERE, YOU LOUSY SONOFABITCH...&quot; He'd hurl the rod into the bay. &quot;TAKE THAT, TOO.&quot; </p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="181" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He married again in 1961, a tall blond model from Chicago, Lee Howard. They'd both been divorced, and they thought they'd make a go. Ted brought her down to the Keys. But he still wasn't staying home: he'd be out at dawn without a word on where he'd go, or what he planned, and then he'd come home, sometimes still without words. Sometimes there was only rage and Lee found she was no match. After two years, she couldn't take it. She said: &quot;I couldn't do anything right. If we went fishing, he would scream at me, call me a ---- and kick the tackle box.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="182" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">So Ted found another woman, one to meet him, fire with fire. Her name was Dolores Wettach, a tall, large-eyed, former Miss Vermont. He spotted her across the aisle on a long plane flight. He was coming from fishing in New Zealand. Dolores had been in Australia, on modeling assignment for <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="182.1">Vogue</em>. He wrote a note: &quot;Who are you?&quot; He wadded it up, tossed it at her. She looked over, tossed one back: &quot;Who are <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="182.3">you</em>?&quot; He tossed: &quot;Mr. Williams, a fisherman,&quot; and later told her his first name was Sam. It wasn't until their third date that she found out he'd done anything but fish. When he found out she was a farm girl who loved the outdoors as much as he, he figured he'd met his match. In a way, he had. She learned to fish, she could hunt, could drink, could curse like a guide. And when they fought, it was toe to toe, and Ted who slammed out of the house. They had a son, John Henry, and daughter, Claudia. But that didn't stop the fights, just as it hadn't with Bobby Jo, the daughter he'd had with Doris. Ted would tell his friends he wasn't cut out for family. He was sick at heart when Bobby Jo left school and didn't go to college. He would seethe when any woman let him know that he'd have to change. What the hell did they want? When Dolores became his third divorce, Ted was through with marriage.</p><hr data-node-id="183" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="184" class="body-dropcap css-vg8se3 emevuu60"><strong data-redactor-tag="strong" data-node-id="184.0">Ted made the Hall of Fame</strong> in 1966. His old enemies, the writers, gave him the largest vote ever. So Ted went north to Cooperstown, and gave a short speed outside the Hall. Then he went back to Florida. He never went inside. They gave him a copy of his plaque. It listed his .406 year, his batting titles, slugging titles, total bases, walks, home runs. It didn't say anything about the wars, the dream, the rage, the cost. But how much can a plaque say?<br data-node-id="184.2"/></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="185" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">There are no statistics on fans, how they felt, what they took from the game. How many of their days did Ted turn around? How many days did he turn to occasions? And not just with hits: there was a special sound from a crowd when Ted got his pitch, turned on the ball, whipped his bat in that perfect arc -- and missed. It was a murmurous rustle, as thousands at once let breath escape, gathered themselves, and leaned forward again. To see Ted suffer a <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="185.1">third</em> strike was an event four times more rare, and more remarkable, than seeing him get a hit. When Ted retired, some owners feared for attendance in the <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="185.3">league</em>. In Boston, where millions came through the years to cheer, to boo, to care what he did, there was an accretion of memory so bright, bittersweet, and strong that when he left, the light was gone. And Fenway was left with a lesser game.</p><section data-embed="pullquote" data-lazy-id="P0-39" data-node-id="186" class="embed"><blockquote data-theme-key="pullquote" class="css-1eiql25 e1pe3zr91"><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-before" class="css-5esr6z eagam8p0"></span><blockquote class="css-xw57yn e1pe3zr90">His old enemies, the writers, gave him the largest vote ever. </blockquote><span aria-hidden="true" data-theme-key="title-design-element-after" class="css-0 eagam8p1"></span></blockquote></section><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="187" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">And what was Ted left with? Well, there was pride. He'd done, he felt, the hardest thing in sport: by God, he hit the ball. And there was pride in his new life: he had his name on more rods and reels, hunting guns, tackle boxes, jackets, boots, and bats than any man in the world. He studied fishing like no other man, and lent to it his fame and grace, his discerning eye. He had his tournament wins and trophies, a fishing book and fishing movies, and he got his thousand of the Big Three. Jimmy Albright says to this day: &quot;Best all around, the best is Ted.&quot; But soon there were scores of boats on the bay, and not so many fish. And even the Miramichi had no pools with salmon wall to wall. And Ted walked away from the tournaments. There wasn't the feeling of sport in them, or respect for the fish anymore. Somehow it had changed. Or maybe it was Ted. </p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="188" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Last year, Ted and Lou went up to Cooperstown together. This was for the unveiling of a statue of the Kid. There are many plaques in the Hall of Fame, but only two statues: just the Babe and him. And Ted went into the Hall this time, pulled the sheet of his statue and his looked at his young self in the finish of that perfect swing. He looked and he looked, with the crowd got quiet, and the strobes stopped flashing. And when he tried to speak, he wept.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="189" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;HEY, WHERE THE HELL IS HE?&quot; It's after 4:00, and Ted's getting hungry. &quot;I'M GONNA CALL HIM.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="190" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Lou says, &quot;Don't be ugly.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="191" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;I'm not ugly,&quot; Ted insists, but quietly. He dials, and bends to look at me. &quot;Hey, if this guy doesn't come, you can eat. You wanna eat here?&quot; Then to the phone: &quot;WHERE THE HELL ARE YOU?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="192" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Ted, don't be mean.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="193" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;I'm not. YEAH. TOMORROW? WELL, OKAY BUDDY.&quot; Ted has had a successful phone conversation. Quick, and to the point.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="194" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Awright, you can eat. Hey, sweetie, take him up so he can see.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="195" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">There are no mementos in the living room, but Lou has put a few special things in a little room upstairs. Most of the pictures have to do with Ted, but the warmth of the room, and its character, have to do with Louise. This is no shrine. It is a room for right now, a room they walk through every day, and a handsome little place, too. Now it is filled with her quiet energy. &quot;Here's Ted Williams when I met him,&quot; she says. &quot;And if that isn't gorgeous, I'll eat my hat.&quot; And here's an old photo of Lou in shorts, with a fly rod, looking fragile next to a tarpon she pulled from Florida Bay. She does not seem fragile now. She is spry and able. She has been with Ted ten years straight, and that speaks volumes for her strength and agility. She gets angry sometimes that people do not credit Ted with tenderness -- &quot;You don't know him,&quot; she says, and her voice has a surprising edge -- but she also knows he'll seldom show it. So here she shows a lonely young Ted with a little suitcase, off to flight school. Here's Ted and Tom Yawkey, and look: Mr. Yawkey had pictures of Ted behind him, too. &quot;Here he is in Korea,&quot; says Louise. &quot;You know, when he landed that place, the blood was pouring from his ears. I have to tell people that...because he's so <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="195.1">loud</em>. Big, too.&quot; Lou picks up a cushion of a window seat. There are pictures beneath. &quot;See, he's done so many things....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="196" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Hey, you want a drink?&quot; Ted is calling. &quot;TED WILLIAMS IS GONNA HAVE A DRINK.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="197" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Soon he flops into his chair with a tumbler, and hands over a videotape. He wants it in the VCR. He says: &quot;This is the most wonderful guy. Hell of a guy. Bill Ziegler. I got him into the majors....&quot; That was when Ted came back in '69 to manage the Senators. Bill Ziegler was the trainer.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="198" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;So he had a son and named him Ted Williams Ziegler. You're gonna see him now. IS IT IN? HEY, YOU LISTENING?&quot; The tape shows Ziegler's two sons batting. Ziegler sends the tapes for analysis. The sound track sends out a steady percussion: <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="198.1">thwack...thwack...thwack</em>. Both boys get wood on the ball. &quot;I'm gonna show you the first tape he sent, and I'm gonna ask what's the difference. See this kid, I told him his hips, he's got to get them OPEN.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="199" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">From the kitchen, Lou protests: &quot;Ted! Not now. Wait for me!&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="200" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;SEE?....&quot; <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="200.1">Thwack</em>. &quot;Ground ball. A little slow with his bands.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="201" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">From Lou: &quot;Okay, okay, I don't know nothin'.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="202" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;HANDS THROUGH!&quot; <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="202.1">Thwack</em>. &quot;Center field, always to center, see where his hips are pointed? He's got to [<em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="202.3">thwack</em>] OPEN 'EM UP.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="203" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">From Lou, coming in, wiping her hands as she watches: &quot;He doesn't step into it like Ted Williams.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="204" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted pretends he doesn't hear. &quot;Hips come through OPEN....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="205" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;He doesn't bring his hands around like you do, honey.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="206" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Yeah, he's got to. GROUND BALL! See, when I'M up&quot; -- and now Ted takes his stance in the living room -- &quot;I'm grindin'....&quot; Now his hands are working. &quot;I got the bands cocked. <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="206.1">COCKED!</em>&quot; And here's the pitch. <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="206.3">&quot;BAMMMM!&quot;</em> says Ted, as he takes his cut and asks: &quot;We got Bill Ziegler's number? WHERE'S HIS NUMBER?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="207" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted is yelling on the phone in the kitchen, and Lou is in the living room, fitting her thoughts to small silences. &quot;When Ted talks [<em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="207.1">thwack</em>] it's always right now....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="208" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;BILL, I WANNA SEE HIM ON HIS FRONT FOOT MORE, AND THE HANDS QUICK, <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="208.1">QUICK</em>....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="209" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;You know, the baseball players...it's not macho, they're just...athletes, just beautiful boys....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="210" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted hangs up and throws himself into his chair: &quot;AWRIGHT, MAJOR LEAGUE! LET'S SET IT UP.&quot; That means dinner. Lou's cooking Chinese. Ted's still watching Ziegler's kids. &quot;Ground ball. You don't make history hittin' 'em on he ground, boys.&quot; Now he pulls away from the TV. &quot;Sweetie,&quot; he sings playfully. &quot;We got any sake-o?&quot; Lou sings, &quot;Not tonight-eo.&quot; Ted sings: &quot;Well, where's the wine-o?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="211" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Lou says grace while all hold hands. Then we set to food, and Ted is major league. &quot;It's good, huh?&quot; he says between mouthfuls. &quot;Well, isn't it? HEY! Aren't you gonna finish that rice?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="212" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">He's finished fast and back in his chair. &quot;We got any sweets?&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="213" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">A little album on the coffee table has pictures from Christmas. John Henry gave his letter of acceptance from Bates as his present to Ted. It's got Ted thinking now about the car he's got to buy so John Henry can take a car to school. &quot;Got to have a car....&quot; He's thinking aloud so Louise can check this out. &quot;Course, there's gonna have to be rules....&quot; He's working it over in his mind, and he muses: &quot;Maybe say that other than school...he can't take the car if his mother says no....&quot; Lou is in a chair across the room. She'd nodding. &quot;HAVE to be rules,&quot; Ted says, &quot;so he doesn't just slam out of the house...slam our and JUMP IN THE CAR....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="214" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Something has turned in his gut, and his face is working, growing harder. There's a mean glitter in his eye, and he's thinking of his elder daughter, walking away from him....</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="215" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;SLAM OUT...LIKE MY DAUGHTER USED TO....&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="216" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">His teeth are clenched and the words are spat. It's like he's turned inward to face something we cannot see. It is a fearsome sight, this big man, forward, stiff in his chair, hurling ugly words at his vision of pain...I feel I should leave the room, but too late.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="217" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60"><em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="217.0">&quot;...THAT BURNED ME...&quot;</em></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="218" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">The switch is on. Lou calls it the Devil in him.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="219" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60"><em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="219.0">&quot;...A PAIN IN MY HAIRY RECTUM!&quot;</em></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="220" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Nice,&quot; says Lou. She is fighting for him. She has not flinched.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="221" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Well, DID,&quot; he says through clenched teeth. <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="221.1">&quot;AND MAKES YOU HATE BROADS!...&quot;</em></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="222" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;Ted. Stop.&quot; But Ted is gone.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="223" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60"><em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="223.0">&quot;...HATE GOD!...&quot;</em></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="224" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;TED!&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="225" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60"><em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="225.0">&quot;...HATE LIFE!&quot;</em></p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="226" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;TED!...JUST...STOP!&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="227" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;DON'T YOU TELL ME TO STOP. DON'T YOU <em data-redactor-tag="em" data-node-id="227.1">EVER</em> TELL ME TO STOP.&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="228" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Lou's mouth twists up slightly, and she snorts: &quot;HAH!&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="229" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">And that does it. They've beaten it, or Lou has, or it's just gone away. Ted sinks back in his chair. His jaw is unclenched. He grins shyly. &quot;You know, I love this girl like I never...&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="230" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Lou sits back, too, and laughs.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="231" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;SHE'S IN TRAINING,&quot; Ted says, &quot;I'M TEACHIN' HER...&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="232" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;He sure is,&quot; Lou says, like it's banter, but her voice is limp. She heads back to the kitchen, and Ted follows her with his eyes.</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="233" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Then he finds me on his couch, and he tries to sneer through his grin: &quot;WHEN ARE YOU LEAVING? HUH?</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="234" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;...JESUS. YOU'RE LIKE THE GODDAMN RUSSIAN SECRET POLICE!</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="235" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">&quot;...OKAY, BYE! YEAH, SURE, GOODBYE!&quot;</p><p data-journey-content="true" data-node-id="236" class="css-i9p093 emevuu60">Ted walks me out to the driveway. As I start the car, Lou's face is a smile in the window, and Ted is bent at his belly, grabbing their new dalmatian puppy, tickling it with his big hands while the dog rolls and paws the air. And as I ease the car into gear, I hear Ted's voice behind, cooing, very quiet now: &quot;Do I love this little dog, huh?....Yes, this little shittin' dog....Yes, yes I love you....Yes, I do.&quot;</p><hr data-node-id="237" class="css-18pb4rg emevuu60"/><div data-journey-blur="partial" data-ad-exclude="true" class="css-19m4yzp e1jy25xv0"></div><div data-ad-exclude="true" Now in his seventh decade, he had girded his penchants for privacy and ease with a bristle of dos and don'ts that defeat casual intrusion. He's been up for hours, he's ready. He fidgets, awaiting appearance by some other, any other, man with a racket, where upon Ted bellows, before the newcomer can say hello: \"WELL, YOU WANNA PLAY?\" Ted's voice normally emanates with gale and force, even at close range. Apologists attribute this to the ear injury that sent him home from Korea and ended his combat flying career. But Ted can speak softly and hear himself fine, if it's only one friend around. The roar with which he speaks in a public place, or to anyone else, has nothing to do with his hearing. It's your hearing he's worried about.","type":"text"}],"name":"p","attribs":{"data-journey-content":true},"type":"tag"},{"children":[{"data":"Ted Williams can hush a room just by entering. There is a force that boils up from him and commands attention. This he has come to accept as his destiny and his due, just as he came to accept the maddening, if respectful, way his opponents pitched around him (he always seemed to be leading the league in bases on balls), or the way every fan in the ball park seemed always to watch (and comment upon) T. Williams's every move. It was often said Ted would rather play ball in a lab, where fans couldn't see. But he never blamed fans for watching him. His hate was for those who couldn't or wouldn't ","type":"text"},{"children":[{"data":"feel","type":"text"}],"name":"em","attribs":{"data-redactor-tag":"em"},"type":"tag"},{"data":" with him, his effort, his exultation, pride, rage, or sorrow. If they wouldn't share those, then there was his scorn, and he's make them feel that, by God. These days, there are no crowds, but Ted is watched, and why not? What other match could draw a kibitzer's eye when Ted, on the near court, pounds toward the net, slashing the air with his big racket, laughing in triumphant derision as he scores with his killer drop shot, or smacking the ball twenty feet long and roaring, \"SYPHILITIC SON OF A BITCH!\" as he hurls his racket to the clay at his feet?","type":"text"}],"name":"p","attribs":{"data-journey-content":true},"type":"tag"},{"children":[{"data":"And who could say Ted does not mean it be seen when he stops in front of the kibitzers as he and his opponent change sides? \"YOU OKAY?\" Ted wheezes as he yells as his foe. \"HOW D'YA FEEL?...HOW OLD ARE YOU?...JUST WORRIED ABOUT YOUR HEART HA HA HAW.\" Ted turns and winks, mops his face. He does not talk about his personal life. \"Why the hell should I?\"","type":"text"}],"name":"p","attribs":{"data-journey-content":true},"type":"tag"},{"children":[{"data":"His standing in the worlds of baseball and fishing would not net him an invitation a night, but he does not go to dinners. One reason is he does not wear ties, and probably hasn't suffered one five times in a quarter century. Neither does he go to parties, where he'd have to stand around, with a drink in his hand, \"listening to a lot of bullshit.\" No, he'd rather watch TV.","type":"text"}],"name":"p","attribs":{"data-journey-content":true},"type":"tag"},{"children":[{"data":"He does not go to restaurants, and the reasons are several: They make a fuss, and the owner or cook's on his neck like gnat. At restaurants you wait, wait, ","type":"text"},{"children":[{"data":"wait","type":"text"}],"name":"em","attribs":{"data-redactor-tag":"em"},"type":"tag"},{"data":". Restaurants have little chairs and tables, no place for elbows, arms, knees, feet. At restaurants there's never enough food. Lastly, restaurants charge a lot, and Ted doesn't toss money around. (A few years ago he decided $2.38 was top price for a pound of beef. For more than a year, he honed his technique on chuck roast and stew meat. Only an incipient boycott by his friends, frequent dinner guests, finally shook his resolve.)","type":"text"}],"name":"p","attribs":{"data-journey-content":true},"type":"tag"},{"children":[{"data":"The last reason is seized upon unkindly by restaurateurs in Islamorada and nearby Keys: \"No, he doesn't come in. And this time around, no one was going to try to blow a fastball by him. Cronin was having an off year and Double-X Foxx was getting old and would never again be batting champ. So the pressure fell to Ted. If they pitched around him and he got a walk, that wasn't enough, the Sox needed hits. If he got a hit, it should have been a homer. A coven of bleacherites started riding Ted. And why not? They could always get a rise. Sometimes he'd yell back. One day in July, a fan in left was riding Ted pretty hard. Ted came to bat in the fifth: he took a strange stance and swung late, hit a line drive, but well foul into the left-field seats. He never had a problem with authority. It was drawing his own lines that gave him fits. He had his fears about the mathematics, navigation problems, and instrument work. But at Amherst College, where the Navy started training, he found his mind was able, and he was pleased. And he loved the feel of an airplane. He was good, right from the start. There was coordination in it, and care: those were natural to him. And he was a constant student, always learning in the air. But he was proudest of his gunnery, the way he could hold back until the last pass, then pour out the lead and shred the sleeve. That wasn't study, that was art. He got his wings near the top of his class and signed on as an instructor at Pensacola, Florida. He was happy, and good at his job. Strangely, in uniform, he was freer than before. FISHING? The mothers of Boston pelted the press with angry letters. \"To hell with them,\" Ted said. He didn't come north for two days. And two days later, he was back fishing. Ted had the best tackle, the best reels, best rods, the perfect line, his lures were impeccable. He'd work for hours at a bench in his house, implanting balsa plugs with lead so they'd sail off a spinning rod just so, then settle in the water slowly like fly. He could stand on the bow of a skiff all day, watching the water for signs of fish, and soon he was seeing them before the guides. His casts were quick and long, his power was immense. ","type":"text"}],"name":"p","attribs":{"data-journey-content":true},"type":"tag"},{"children":[{"data":"On a bombing run, north of the 38th parallel, Ted lost sight of the plane ahead. He dropped through clouds, and when he came out, he was much too low. North Koreans sent up a hail of bullets. Ted's place was hit and set afire. The stick stiffened and shook in his hand; his hydraulics were gone. Every warning light was red. The radio quit. A Marine in a nearby F-9 was pointing wildly at Ted's place. He was trying to signal: \"Fire! Bail out!\" But Ted's biggest fear was ejecting; at six three, wedged in as he was, he'd leave his kneecaps under his gauges. So the other pilot led him to a base. Ted hauled his plane into a turn and he felt a shudder of explosion. One of his wheel doors had blown out. Now he was burning below, too. He made for a runway with fire streaming thirty feet behind. Koreans in a village saw his plane and ran for their lives. Only one wheel came down; he had no dive breaks, air flaps, nothing to slow the plane. He hit the concrete at 225 miles per hour and slid for almost a mile, while he mashed the useless brakes and screamed, \"STOP YOU DIRTY SONOFABITCH STOP STOP STOP.\" When the F-9 stopped skidding, he somersaulted out the hatch and slammed his helmet to the ground. Two Marines grabbed him on the tarmac, and walked him away as the plane burned to char. ","type":"text"},{"children":[],"name":"br","attribs":{},"type":"tag"}],"name":"p","attribs":{"data-journey-content":true},"type":"tag"},{"children":[{"data":"Ted Williams was the greatest old hitter. In two months, upon return from Korea, he batted .407 and hit a home run once in every seven at bats. For the next two years, he led the league (.345 and .356), but injuries and walks robbed him of the titles: he didn't get the minimum four hundred at bats. In 1956, he lost the title in the season's last week to twenty-four-year-old Mickey Mantle (who finished .353 to Ted's .345). The next year, Mantle had an even better season, but Ted, at age thirty-nine, pulled away and won, at .388, more than twenty points ahead of Mantle, more than sixty points ahead of anyone else. With five more hits (say, the leg hits that a younger man would get), it would have been .400. As it was, it stood at the highest average since his own .406, sixteen years before. In 1958, Ted battled for the crown again, this time with a teammate, Pete Runnels. They were even in September, but then, once again, Ted pulled way to win at .328. ","expand":"","crop":"original"},"type":"tag","response":{"captionDom":{"children":[{"data":"Williams prepares for takeoff. ","type":"text"}],"type":"root"}}},{"children":[{"data":"He accomplished these prodigies despite troubles that would have made most men quit. In 1954, he made spring training for the first time in three years, but he wasn't on the field a minute before he fell and broke his collarbone. He was out six weeks and had steel bar wired into his clavicle. (First day back, twin bill in Detroit: two home runs, eight-for-nine, seven RBIs.) In 1955, Doris alleged in divorce court that he'd treated her with \"extreme cruelty\" and constant profane abuse. Boston papers ran the story under two-inch headlines: TED GETS DIVORCE, with a \"box score\" on the money, the house, the car, and \"Mrs. Ted's\" custody of Bobby Jo. In 1956, Ted came forth with his Great Expectorations. In a scoreless game with the Yankees, in front of Fenway's biggest crowd since World War II, he was booed for an error, and he let fans know what he thought of them: he spat toward the right-field stands and spat toward the left, and when fans rained more boos on his head, he leaped out of the dugout and sprayed all around. \"Oh, no, this is a bad scene,\" Curt Gowdy, the Sox broadcaster, mourned to his microphone. Tom Yawkey heard the game on the radio, and Ted got a $5,000 fine (tying another Babe Ruth record). Boston writers said Ted ought to quit. But Ted was in the next game, Family Night, and at his appearance, fans gave him a five-minute ovation. (He then hit a home run in the bottom of the eighth and clapped his hand over his mouth as he scored the winning run.) In 1957, grippe knocked him flat and stuck him in his hotel for seventeen days in September. He came back and hit four consecutive home runs. In 1958, ptomaine from bad oysters wrecked opening day, then he injured an ankle, pulled a muscle in his side, and hurt his wrist twice. In September, after a called third strike, Ted threw his bat and watched in horror as it sailed to the stands and clonked a gray-haired lady on the head. Ted sat in tears in the dugout and had to be ordered to his place in left field. But over the next twenty at-bats, he hit .500.","type":"text"}],"name":"p","attribs":{"data-journey-content":true},"type":"tag"},{"children":[{"data":"Now the switch in his gut was always on. The Red Sox gave him a single room and barred the press from the clubhouse for two hours before each game. But it wasn't outside annoyance that was fueling Ted's rage. He'd wake up in the middle of the night, screaming obscenities in the dark. He kept himself alone and pushed away affection. There were plenty of women who would have loved to help. But Ted would say: \"WOMEN?\" and then he'd grab his crotch. \"ALL THEY WANT IS WHAT I GOT RIGHT HERE.\" Now the press didn't cover just explosions on the field. The ","type":"text"},{"children":[{"data":"American","type":"text"}],"name":"em","attribs":{"data-redactor-tag":"em"},"type":"tag"},{"data":" wrote him up for shredding a telephone book all over the floor when a hotel maid failed to clean his room. \"Now tell me some more,\" wrote Austen Lake, \"about Ted's big, charitable, long-suffering spirit.\" Roger Kahn reported a scene when Ted was asked about Billy Klaus, the shortstop who was coming back after a bad year. \"You're asking ME about a BAD YEAR?...OLD T.S.W., HE DON'T HAVE BAD YEARS.\"","type":"text"}],"name":"p","attribs":{"data-journey-content":true},"type":"tag"},{"children":[{"data":"But old Ted had a terrible year in 1959. A pain in his neck turned to stiffness, and he was in traction for three weeks. When he came out, he could barely look at the pitcher. He average languished below .300 for the first time in his career. For the first time, he was benched for not hitting. The sight of the Kid at plate was pathetic; even the papers softened. They started summing up his career, treating him like an old building menaced by the wrecking ball. He finished at .254 and went to Tom Yawkey. \"Why don't you just wrap it up?\" Yawkey said, and Ted started to boil. No one was going to make him retire. Ted said he meant to play, and Yawkey, who loved the Kid, offered to renew his contract: $125,000, the highest ever. No, Ted said, he'd had a lousy year and he wanted a cut. At forty-two, Ted finished his year with twenty-nine homers and .316. Talk revived that Ted might be back. But this was really quits. On his last day at Fenway, a headline cried: WHAT WILL WE DO WITHOUT TED? And though the day was dreary and the season without hope, ten thousand came out to cheer him and hear him say goodbye. There was another check for the Jimmy Fund and, this time, a silver bowl. And Ted made a speech that said, despite all, he felt lucky to play for these fans. And when he came up in the eighth and they stood to cheer, he showed them what Ted Williams could do. He hit a Jack Fisher fastball into the bullpen in right field. And he thought about tipping his cap as he rounded first but he couldn't, even then, couldn't forget, so he ran it straight into the dugout, and wouldn't come out for a bow.
